       <tr>
        <td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;padding-top:0">
         <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
          <ul>
           <li class="nv-view">
            <a href="/wiki/Template:Years_in_film" title="Template:Years in film">
             <abbr title="View this template">
              v
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="nv-talk">
            <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Years_in_film" title="Template talk:Years in film">
             <abbr title="Discuss this template">
              t
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="nv-edit">
            <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Years_in_film&amp;action=edit">
             <abbr title="Edit this template">
              e
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Although the start of the
       <b>
        history of film
       </b>
       is not clearly defined, the commercial, public screening of ten of
       <a href="/wiki/Auguste_and_Louis_Lumi%C3%A8re" title="Auguste and Louis Lumière">
        Lumière brothers
       </a>
       ' short films in Paris on 28 December 1895 can be regarded as the breakthrough of projected
       <a href="/wiki/Cinematography" title="Cinematography">
        cinematographic
       </a>
       motion pictures. There had been earlier cinematographic results and screenings but these lacked either the quality or the momentum that propelled the cinématographe Lumière into a worldwide success.
      </p>
      <p>
       Soon film production companies were established all over the world. The first decade of motion picture saw film moving from a novelty to an established mass entertainment industry.
      </p>
      <p>
       The earliest films were in black and white, under a minute long and without recorded sound.
      </p>
      <p>
       During the 1890s films became several minutes long and started to consist of several shots. The first film studios were built in 1897. The first rotating camera for taking
       <a href="/wiki/Panning_(camera)" title="Panning (camera)">
        panning shots
       </a>
       was built in 1898. Special effects were introduced and film continuity, involving action moving from one sequence into another, began to be used.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the 1900s, continuity of action across successive shots was achieved and the first close-up shot was introduced (that some claim
       <a href="/wiki/D._W._Griffith" title="D. W. Griffith">
        D. W. Griffith
       </a>
       invented). Most films of this period were what came to be called "chase films". The first successful permanent theatre showing only films was "The Nickelodeon" in Pittsburgh in 1905. The first feature length multi-reel film was a 1906 Australian production. By 1910, actors began to receive screen credit for their roles and the way to the creation of film stars was opened. Regular newsreels were exhibited from 1910 and soon became a popular way for finding out the news. From about 1910, American films had the largest share of the market in Australia and in all European countries except France.
      </p>
      <p>
       New film techniques were introduced in this period including the use of artificial lighting, fire effects and
       <a href="/wiki/Low-key_lighting" title="Low-key lighting">
        low-key lighting
       </a>
       (i.e. lighting in which most of the frame is dark) for enhanced atmosphere during sinister scenes. As films grew longer, specialist writers were employed to simplify more complex stories derived from novels or plays into a form that could be contained on one reel and be easier to be understood by the audience –  an audience that was new to this form of storytelling.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genres" title="Genres">
        Genres
       </a>
       began to be used as categories; the main division was into comedy and drama but these categories were further subdivided. During the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_World_War" title="First World War">
        First World War
       </a>
       there was a complex transition for the film industry. The exhibition of films changed from short one-reel programs to feature films. Exhibition venues became larger and began charging higher prices. By 1914, continuity cinema was the established mode of commercial cinema. One of the advanced continuity techniques involved an accurate and smooth transition from one shot to another.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_Wark_Griffith" title="David Wark Griffith">
        D. W. Griffith
       </a>
       had the highest standing among American directors in the industry, because of the dramatic excitement he conveyed to the audience through his films. The American film industry, or "
       <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_the_United_States" title="Cinema of the United States">
        Hollywood
       </a>
       ", as it was becoming known after its new geographical center in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hollywood,_Los_Angeles" title="Hollywood, Los Angeles">
        Hollywood, a neighborhood in Los Angeles, California
       </a>
       , gained the position it has held, more or less, ever since: film factory for the world and exporting its product to most countries. By the 1920s, the United States reached what is still its era of greatest-ever output, producing an average of 800
       <i>
        feature
       </i>
       films annually,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-filmsite.org_1-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-filmsite.org-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       or 82% of the global total (Eyman, 1997). During late 1927, Warner's released
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Jazz_Singer" title="The Jazz Singer">
         The Jazz Singer
        </a>
       </i>
       , with the first synchronized dialogue (and singing) in a feature film. By the end of 1929, Hollywood was almost all-talkie, with several competing sound systems (soon to be standardized). Sound saved the Hollywood studio system in the face of the
       <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">
        Great Depression
       </a>
       (Parkinson, 1995).
      </p>
      <p>
       The desire for wartime propaganda created a renaissance in the film industry in Britain, with realistic war dramas. The onset of American involvement in World War II also brought a proliferation of films as both
       <a href="/wiki/Patriotism" title="Patriotism">
        patriotism
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">
        propaganda
       </a>
       . The
       <a href="/wiki/House_Un-American_Activities_Committee" title="House Un-American Activities Committee">
        House Un-American Activities Committee
       </a>
       investigated Hollywood in the early 1950s. During the immediate post-war years the cinematic industry was also threatened by
       <a href="/wiki/Television" title="Television">
        television
       </a>
       and the increasing popularity of the medium meant that some film theatres would bankrupt and close. The 1950s was a 'Golden Age' for non-English
       <a href="/wiki/World_cinema" title="World cinema">
        world cinema
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Roundhay_Garden_Scene" title="Roundhay Garden Scene">
         Roundhay Garden Scene
        </a>
       </i>
       is an 1888
       <a href="/wiki/Short_film" title="Short film">
        short
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Silent_film" title="Silent film">
        silent film
       </a>
       recorded by French inventor
       <a href="/wiki/Louis_Le_Prince" title="Louis Le Prince">
        Louis Le Prince
       </a>
       . It is believed to be the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Film" title="History of Film">
        oldest surviving film
       </a>
       in existence, as noted by the
       <a href="/wiki/Guinness_World_Records" title="Guinness World Records">
        <i>
         Guinness Book of Records
        </i>
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The film
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sortie_de_l%27usine_Lumi%C3%A8re_de_Lyon" title="Sortie de l'usine Lumière de Lyon">
         Sortie de l'usine Lumière de Lyon
        </a>
       </i>
       (1895) by French
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_Lumi%C3%A8re" title="Louis Lumière">
        Louis Lumière
       </a>
       is considered the "first true motion picture "
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encarta_3-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Encarta-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

          <span class="toctext">
           1980s
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26">
         <a href="#1990s">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           13
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           1990s
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27">
         <a href="#Recent_years">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           14
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           Recent years
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28">
         <a href="#See_also">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           15
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           See also
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29">
         <a href="#References">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           16
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           References
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30">
         <a href="#Further_reading">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           17
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           Further reading
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31">

          </span>
          <span class="toctext">

          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
       </ul>
      </div>
      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Precursors">
        Precursors
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Precursors">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Precursors_of_film" title="Precursors of film">
        Precursors of film
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Much of
       <a href="/wiki/Film" title="Film">
        film
       </a>
       as an art form grew out of several earlier traditions in the fields of (oral)
       <a href="/wiki/Storytelling" title="Storytelling">
        storytelling
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Literature" title="Literature">
        literature
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Theatre" title="Theatre">
        theatre
       </a>
       and visual
       <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">
        art
       </a>
       . Examples of quite similar forms of art and entertainment that already featured moving and/or projected images include:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Shadowgraphy_(performing_art)" title="Shadowgraphy (performing art)">
         Shadowgraphy
        </a>
        (probably in practice since prehistoric times)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Camera_obscura" title="Camera obscura">
         Camera obscura
        </a>
        (a natural phenomenon that has possibly been used as an artistic aid since prehistoric times)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Shadow_play" title="Shadow play">
         Shadow puppetry
        </a>
        (possibly originated around 200 BCE in Central Asia, India, Indonesia or China)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Magic_lantern" title="Magic lantern">
         Magic lantern
        </a>
        (developed in the 1650s, preceded by some incidental and/or inferior
        <a href="/wiki/Projector" title="Projector">
         projectors
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        stroboscopic "
        <a href="/wiki/Persistence_of_vision" title="Persistence of vision">
         persistence of vision
        </a>
        " animation devices (
        <a href="/wiki/Phenakistiscope" title="Phenakistiscope">
         phénakisticope
        </a>
        since 1832,
        <a href="/wiki/Zoetrope" title="Zoetrope">
         zoetrope
        </a>
        since 1866,
        <a href="/wiki/Flip_book" title="Flip book">
         flip book
        </a>
        since 1868)
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Some ancient sightings of gods and spirits are thought to have been conjured up by means of (concave) mirrors, camera obscura or unknown projectors. By the 16th century
       <a href="/wiki/Necromancy" title="Necromancy">
        necromantic
       </a>
       ceremonies and the conjuring of ghostly apparitions by charlatan "magicians" and "witches" seemed commonplace.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The very first magic lantern shows seem to have continued this tradition with images of Death, monsters and other scary figures. Around 1790 this was developed into multi-media ghost shows known as
       <a href="/wiki/Phantasmagoria" title="Phantasmagoria">
        phantasmagoria
       </a>
       that could feature mechanical slides, rear projection, mobile projectors,
       <a href="/wiki/Superimposition" title="Superimposition">
        superimposition
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Dissolve_(filmmaking)" title="Dissolve (filmmaking)">
        dissolves
       </a>
       , live actors, smoke (sometimes to project images upon), odors, sounds and even electric shocks. Similar spooky subject matter would form the basis of the
       <a href="/wiki/Horror_film" title="Horror film">
        horror film
       </a>
       genre that emerged very soon after the introduction of cinema.
      </p>
      <p>
       Moving images were possibly projected with the magic lantern since its invention;
       <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">
        Christiaan Huygens
       </a>
       ' 1659 sketches for slides show a skeleton taking his skull off his neck and placing it back. Techniques to add motion to the painted glass slides were described since circa 1700. These usually involved parts (for instance limbs) painted on one or more extra pieces of glass, moved by hand or small mechanisms across a stationary slide which showed the rest of the picture.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rossell2005_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rossell2005-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       While the first magic lantern images seem to have been intended to scare audiences, soon all sorts of subjects appeared and the lantern was not only used for storytelling but also for education. In the 19th century several new and popular magic lantern techniques were developed, including
       <a href="/wiki/Dissolving_views" title="Dissolving views">
        dissolving views
       </a>
       and several types of mechanical slides that created dazzling abstract effects (chromatrope, et cetera) or that showed for instance falling snow, or the planets and their moons revolving.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_period">
        Early period
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early period">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Silent_film" title="Silent film">
        Silent film
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Sound_film" title="Sound film">
        Sound film
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kinetoscope.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="719" data-file-width="500" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Kinetoscope.jpg/150px-Kinetoscope.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Kinetoscope.jpg/225px-Kinetoscope.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Kinetoscope.jpg/300px-Kinetoscope.jpg 2x" width="150"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kinetoscope.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Interior view of
         <a href="/wiki/Kinetoscope" title="Kinetoscope">
          Kinetoscope
         </a>
         with peephole viewer at top of cabinet.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In the 1890s, films were seen mostly via temporary storefront spaces and traveling exhibitors or as acts in vaudeville programs. A film could be under a minute long and would usually present a single scene, authentic or staged, of everyday life, a public event, a sporting event or
       <a href="/wiki/Slapstick" title="Slapstick">
        slapstick
       </a>
       . There was little to no cinematic technique, the film was usually black and white and it was without sound.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fratelli_Lumiere.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="356" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Fratelli_Lumiere.jpg/200px-Fratelli_Lumiere.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Fratelli_Lumiere.jpg/300px-Fratelli_Lumiere.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/93/Fratelli_Lumiere.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fratelli_Lumiere.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Brothers Auguste and Louis Lumière
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The novelty of realistic moving photographs was enough for a motion picture industry to blossom before the end of the century, in countries around the world. "The Cinema" was to offer a cheaper, simpler way of providing entertainment to the masses. Filmmakers could record actors' performances, which then could be shown to audiences around the world. Travelogues would bring the sights of far-flung places, with movement, directly to spectators' hometowns. Movies would become the most popular visual art form of the late Victorian age.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Wintergarten_theatre" title="Berlin Wintergarten theatre">
        Berlin Wintergarten theater
       </a>
       hosted an early movie presentation in front of an audience, shown by the
       <a href="/wiki/Max_Skladanowsky" title="Max Skladanowsky">
        Skladanowsky brothers
       </a>
       in 1895. The
       <a href="/wiki/Melbourne_Athenaeum" title="Melbourne Athenaeum">
        Melbourne Athenaeum
       </a>
       started to screen movies in 1896.
       <a href="/wiki/Movie_theater" title="Movie theater">
        Movie theaters
       </a>
       became popular entertainment venues and social hubs in the early 20th century, much like
       <a href="/wiki/Cabaret" title="Cabaret">
        cabarets
       </a>
       and other theaters.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Le_Voyage_dans_la_lune.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="264" data-file-width="257" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Le_Voyage_dans_la_lune.jpg/220px-Le_Voyage_dans_la_lune.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/04/Le_Voyage_dans_la_lune.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Le_Voyage_dans_la_lune.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A scene from
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/A_Trip_to_the_Moon" title="A Trip to the Moon">
           A Trip to the Moon
          </a>
         </i>
         (1902) by
         <a href="/wiki/Georges_M%C3%A9li%C3%A8s" title="Georges Méliès">
          Georges Méliès
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Until 1927, motion pictures were produced without sound. This era is referred to as the
       <a href="/wiki/Silent_film" title="Silent film">
        silent era of film
       </a>
       . To enhance the viewers' experience, silent films were commonly accompanied by live musicians in an orchestra, a theatre organ, and sometimes
       <a href="/wiki/Sound_effect" title="Sound effect">
        sound effects
       </a>
       and even commentary spoken by the showman or projectionist. In most countries,
       <a href="/wiki/Intertitle" title="Intertitle">
        intertitles
       </a>
       came to be used to provide dialogue and narration for the film, thus dispensing with narrators, but in Japanese cinema human narration remained popular throughout the silent era. The technical problems were resolved by 1923.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Illustrated_song" title="Illustrated song">
        Illustrated songs
       </a>
       were a notable exception to this trend that began in 1894 in
       <a href="/wiki/Vaudeville" title="Vaudeville">
        vaudeville
       </a>
       houses and persisted as late as the late 1930s in film theaters.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_House_of_Witmark_7-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-The_House_of_Witmark-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Live performance or sound recordings were paired with hand-colored glass
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magic_lantern_(projector)" title="Magic lantern (projector)">
        slides
       </a>
       projected through
       <a href="/wiki/Stereopticon" title="Stereopticon">
        stereopticons
       </a>
       and similar devices. In this way, song narrative was illustrated through a series of slides whose changes were simultaneous with the narrative development. The main purpose of illustrated songs was to encourage
       <a href="/wiki/Sheet_music" title="Sheet music">
        sheet music
       </a>
       sales, and they were highly successful with sales reaching into the millions for a single song. Later, with the birth of film, illustrated songs were used as filler material preceding films and during
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Film_reel" title="Film reel">
        reel changes
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Sounds_of_Early_Cinema_ch._14_8-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-The_Sounds_of_Early_Cinema_ch._14-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The 1914
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Photo-Drama_of_Creation" title="The Photo-Drama of Creation">
         The Photo-Drama of Creation
        </a>
       </i>
       was a non-commercial attempt to combine the motion picture with a combination of slides and synchronize the resulting moving picture with audio. The film included hand-painted slides as well as other previously used techniques. Simultaneously playing the audio while the film was being played with a projector was required. Produced by the
       <a href="/wiki/Watch_Tower_Bible_and_Tract_Society_of_Pennsylvania" title="Watch Tower Bible and Tract Society of Pennsylvania">
        Watch Tower Bible and Tract Society of Pennsylvania
       </a>
       (Jehovah's Witnesses), this eight–hour bible drama was being shown in 80 cities every day and almost eight million people in the United States and Canada saw the presentation.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Birth_of_movies">
        Birth of movies
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Birth of movies">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The first eleven years of motion pictures show the cinema moving from a novelty to an established large-scale entertainment industry. The films represent a movement from films consisting of one shot, completely made by one person with a few assistants, towards films several minutes long consisting of several shots, which were made by large companies in something like industrial conditions.
      </p>
      <p>
       The year 1900 marks the emergence of the first motion pictures that can be considered as "films" –  at this point, film-makers begin to introduce basic editing techniques and film narrative.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Invention_and_advancement_of_the_camera">
        Invention and advancement of the camera
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Invention and advancement of the camera">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Melstud2.gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="594" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Melstud2.gif/200px-Melstud2.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Melstud2.gif/300px-Melstud2.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Melstud2.gif/400px-Melstud2.gif 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Melstud2.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Georges_M%C3%A9li%C3%A8s" title="Georges Méliès">
          Georges Méliès
         </a>
         (left) painting a backdrop in his studio.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Early movie cameras were fastened to the head of their tripod with only simple levelling devices provided. These cameras were thus effectively fixed during the course of the shot, and hence the first camera movements were the result of mounting a camera on a moving vehicle. The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lumi%C3%A8re_brothers" title="Lumière brothers">
        Lumière brothers
       </a>
       shot a scene from the back of a train in 1896.
      </p>
      <p>
       The first rotating camera for taking
       <a href="/wiki/Panning_(camera)" title="Panning (camera)">
        panning shots
       </a>
       was built by
       <a href="/wiki/Robert_W._Paul" title="Robert W. Paul">
        Robert W. Paul
       </a>
       in 1897, on the occasion of
       <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">
        Queen Victoria
       </a>
       's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diamond_Jubilee" title="Diamond Jubilee">
        Diamond Jubilee
       </a>
       . He used his camera to shoot the procession in one shot. His device had the camera mounted on a vertical axis that could be rotated by a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Worm_gear" title="Worm gear">
        worm gear
       </a>
       driven by turning a crank handle, and Paul put it on general sale the next year. Shots taken using such a
       <a href="/wiki/Panning_(camera)" title="Panning (camera)">
        "panning"
       </a>
       head were also referred to as 'panoramas' in the film catalogues of the first decade of the cinema.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Georges_M%C3%A9li%C3%A8s" title="Georges Méliès">
        Georges Méliès
       </a>
       built one of the first film studios in May 1897. It had a glass roof and three glass walls constructed after the model of large studios for still photography, and it was fitted with thin cotton cloths that could be stretched below the roof to diffuse the direct rays of the sun on sunny days.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Beginning in 1896, Méliès would go on to produce, direct, and distribute over 500 short films. The majority of these films were short, one-shot films completed in one take. Méliès drew many comparisons between film and the stage, which was apparent in his work. He realized that film afforded him the ability (via his use of timelapse photography) to "produce visual spectacles not achievable in the theater.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gazetas,_Aristides_2000_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gazetas,_Aristides_2000-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Execution_of_Mary_Stuart" title="The Execution of Mary Stuart">
         The Execution of Mary Stuart
        </a>
       </i>
       , produced by the Edison Company for viewing with the
       <a href="/wiki/Kinetoscope" title="Kinetoscope">
        Kinetoscope
       </a>
       , showed
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mary_Queen_of_Scots" title="Mary Queen of Scots">
        Mary Queen of Scots
       </a>
       being executed in full view of the camera. The effect was achieved by replacing the actor with a dummy for the final shot.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Georges Méliès also utilized this technique in the making of
       <i>
        Escamotage d'un dame chez Robert-Houdin (The Vanishing Lady)
       </i>
       . The woman is seen to vanish through the use of
       <a href="/wiki/Stop_motion" title="Stop motion">
        stop motion
       </a>
       techniques.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Santa_Claus,_1898.gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="231" data-file-width="300" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5b/Santa_Claus%2C_1898.gif/200px-Santa_Claus%2C_1898.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/5/5b/Santa_Claus%2C_1898.gif 1.5x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Santa_Claus,_1898.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A scene inset inside a circular vignette showing a "dream vision" in
         <i>
          Santa Claus
         </i>
         (1899).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The other basic technique for trick cinematography was the
       <a href="/wiki/Multiple_exposure" title="Multiple exposure">
        double exposure
       </a>
       of the film in the camera. This was pioneered by
       <a href="/wiki/George_Albert_Smith_(film_pioneer)" title="George Albert Smith (film pioneer)">
        George Albert Smith
       </a>
       in July 1898 in England. The set was draped in black, and after the main shot, the negative was re-exposed to the overlaid scene. His
       <i>
        The Corsican Brothers
       </i>
       was described in the catalogue of the
       <a href="/wiki/Warwick_Trading_Company" title="Warwick Trading Company">
        Warwick Trading Company
       </a>
       in 1900: "By extremely careful photography the ghost appears *quite transparent*. After indicating that he has been killed by a sword-thrust, and appealing for vengeance, he disappears. A 'vision' then appears showing the fatal duel in the snow.”
      </p>
      <p>
       G.A. Smith also initiated the special effects technique of
       <a href="/wiki/Reverse_motion" title="Reverse motion">
        reverse motion
       </a>
       . He did this by repeating the action a second time, while filming it with an inverted camera, and then joining the tail of the second negative to that of the first.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The first films made using this device were
       <i>
        Tipsy, Topsy, Turvy
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        The Awkward Sign Painter
       </i>
       . The earliest surviving example of this technique is Smith's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_House_That_Jack_Built_(1900_film)" title="The House That Jack Built (1900 film)">
         The House That Jack Built
        </a>
       </i>
       , made before September 1900.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Hepworth" title="Cecil Hepworth">
        Cecil Hepworth
       </a>
       took this technique further, by printing the
       <a href="/wiki/Negative_(photography)" title="Negative (photography)">
        negative
       </a>
       of the forwards motion backwards frame by frame, so producing a print in which the original action was exactly reversed. To do this he built a special printer in which the negative running through a projector was projected into the gate of a camera through a special lens giving a same-size image. This arrangement came to be called a "projection printer", and eventually an "
       <a href="/wiki/Optical_printer" title="Optical printer">
        optical printer
       </a>
       ".
      </p>
      <p>
       The use of different camera speeds also appeared around 1900 in the films of
       <a href="/wiki/Robert_W._Paul" title="Robert W. Paul">
        Robert W. Paul
       </a>
       and Hepworth. Paul shot scenes from
       <i>
        On a Runaway Motor Car through Piccadilly Circus
       </i>
       (1899) with the camera turning very slowly. When the film was projected at the usual 16 frames per second, the scenery appeared to be passing at great speed. Hepworth used the opposite effect in
       <i>
        The Indian Chief and the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seidlitz_powder" title="Seidlitz powder">
         Seidlitz Powder
        </a>
       </i>
       (1901). The Chief's movements are sped up by cranking the camera much faster than 16 frames per second. This gives what we would call a "
       <a href="/wiki/Slow_motion" title="Slow motion">
        slow motion
       </a>
       " effect.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Film_editing_and_continuous_narrative">
        Film editing and continuous narrative
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Film editing and continuous narrative">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The first films to consist of more than one shot appeared toward the end of the 19th century, a notable example was the French film of the life of
       <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">
        Jesus Christ
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">
          <span title="Which movie? (February 2017)">
           specify
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alice_Guy" title="Alice Guy">
        Alice Guy
       </a>
       . These weren't represented as a continuous film, the separate scenes were interspersed with lantern slides, a lecture, and live choral numbers, to increase the running time of the spectacle to about 90 minutes. Another example of this is the reproductions of scenes from the
       <a href="/wiki/Greco-Turkish_War_(1897)" title="Greco-Turkish War (1897)">
        Greco-Turkish war
       </a>
       , made by
       <a href="/wiki/Georges_M%C3%A9li%C3%A8s" title="Georges Méliès">
        Georges Méliès
       </a>
       in 1897. Although each scene was sold separately, they were shown one after the other by the exhibitors. Even Méliès'
       <i>
        Cendrillon (Cinderella)
       </i>
       of 1898 contained no action moving from one shot to the next one. To understand what was going on in the film the audience had to know their stories beforehand, or be told them by a presenter.
      </p>
      <div class="center">
       <div class="thumb tnone">
        <div class="thumbinner" style="width:602px;">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paul3.gif">
          <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="227" data-file-width="606" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/60/Paul3.gif/600px-Paul3.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/6/60/Paul3.gif 1.5x" width="600"/>
         </a>
         <div class="thumbcaption">
          <div class="magnify">
           <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paul3.gif" title="Enlarge">
           </a>
          </div>
          The two scenes making up
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/Come_Along,_Do!" title="Come Along, Do!">
            Come Along, Do!
           </a>
          </i>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Real film continuity, involving action moving from one sequence into another, is attributed to British film pioneer
       <a href="/wiki/Robert_W._Paul" title="Robert W. Paul">
        Robert W. Paul
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Come_Along,_Do!" title="Come Along, Do!">
         Come Along, Do!
        </a>
       </i>
       , made in 1898 and one of the first films to feature more than one shot.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BFIso01_15-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-BFIso01-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the first shot, an elderly couple is outside an
       <a href="/wiki/Art_exhibition" title="Art exhibition">
        art exhibition
       </a>
       having lunch and then follow other people inside through the door. The second shot shows what they do inside. Paul's 'Cinematograph Camera No. 1' of 1895 was the first camera to feature reverse-cranking, which allowed the same film footage to be exposed several times and thereby to create super-positions and
       <a href="/wiki/Multiple_exposure" title="Multiple exposure">
        multiple exposures
       </a>
       . This technique was first used in his 1901 film
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Scrooge,_or,_Marley%27s_Ghost" title="Scrooge, or, Marley's Ghost">
         Scrooge, or, Marley's Ghost
        </a>
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The further development of action continuity in multi-shot films continued in 1899 at the
       <a href="/wiki/Brighton" title="Brighton">
        Brighton
       </a>
       School in
       <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">
        England
       </a>
       . In the latter part of that year,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Albert_Smith_(inventor)" title="George Albert Smith (inventor)">
        George Albert Smith
       </a>
       made
       <i>
        The Kiss in the Tunnel
       </i>
       . This started with a shot from a "phantom ride" at the point at which the train goes into a tunnel, and continued with the action on a set representing the interior of a railway carriage, where a man steals a kiss from a woman, and then cuts back to the phantom ride shot when the train comes out of the tunnel. A month later, the Bamforth company in Yorkshire made a restaged version of this film under the same title, and in this case they filmed shots of a train entering and leaving a tunnel from beside the tracks, which they joined before and after their version of the kiss inside the train compartment.
      </p>
      <div class="center">
       <div class="thumb tnone">
        <div class="thumbinner" style="width:602px;">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Telesc4.gif">
          <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="229" data-file-width="606" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f6/Telesc4.gif/600px-Telesc4.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/f/f6/Telesc4.gif 1.5x" width="600"/>
         </a>
         <div class="thumbcaption">
          <div class="magnify">
           <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Telesc4.gif" title="Enlarge">
           </a>
          </div>
          The first two shots of
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/As_Seen_Through_a_Telescope" title="As Seen Through a Telescope">
            As Seen Through a Telescope
           </a>
          </i>
          (1900), with the telescope POV simulated by the circular mask.
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In 1900, continuity of action across successive shots was definitively established by George Albert Smith and
       <a href="/wiki/James_Williamson_(film_pioneer)" title="James Williamson (film pioneer)">
        James Williamson
       </a>
       , who also worked in
       <a href="/wiki/Brighton" title="Brighton">
        Brighton
       </a>
       . In that year Smith made
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/As_Seen_Through_a_Telescope" title="As Seen Through a Telescope">
         As Seen Through a Telescope
        </a>
       </i>
       , in which the main shot shows street scene with a young man tying the shoelace and then caressing the foot of his girlfriend, while an old man observes this through a telescope. There is then a cut to close shot of the hands on the girl's foot shown inside a black circular mask, and then a cut back to the continuation of the original scene. Even more remarkable is
       <a href="/wiki/James_Williamson_(film_pioneer)" title="James Williamson (film pioneer)">
        James Williamson
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        Attack on a China Mission Station
       </i>
       (1900). The first shot shows Chinese Boxer rebels at the gate; it then cuts to the missionary family in the garden, where a fight ensues. The wife signals to British sailors from the
       <a href="/wiki/Balcony" title="Balcony">
        balcony
       </a>
       , who come and rescue them. The film also used the first "reverse angle" cut in film history.
      </p>
      <p>
       G.A Smith pioneered the use of the close-up shot in his 1900 films
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/As_Seen_Through_a_Telescope" title="As Seen Through a Telescope">
         As Seen Through a Telescope
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Grandma%27s_Reading_Glass" title="Grandma's Reading Glass">
         Grandma's Reading Glass
        </a>
       </i>
       . He further developed the ideas of breaking a scene shot in one place into a series of shots taken from different camera positions over the next couple of years, starting with
       <i>
        The Little Doctors
       </i>
       of 1901. In a series of films he produced at this time, he also introduced the use of subjective and objective point-of-view shots, the creation of dream-time and the use of reversing. He summed up his work in
       <i>
        Mary Jane's Mishap
       </i>
       of 1903, with repeated cuts in to a close shot of a housemaid fooling around, along with superimpositions and other devices, before abandoning film-making to invent the Kinemacolor system of colour cinematography. His films were the first to establish the basics of coherent narrative and what became known as film language, or "
       <a href="/wiki/Film_grammar" title="Film grammar">
        film grammar
       </a>
       ".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BFIso001_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-BFIso001-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BF01_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-BF01-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       James Williamson concentrated on making films taking action from one place shown in one shot to the next shown in another shot in films like
       <i>
        Stop Thief!
       </i>
       , made in 1901, and many others. He also experimented with the close-up, and made perhaps the most extreme one of all in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Big_Swallow" title="The Big Swallow">
         The Big Swallow
        </a>
       </i>
       , when his character approaches the camera and appears to swallow it. These two film makers of the Brighton School also pioneered the editing of the film; they tinted their work with color and used trick photography to enhance the narrative. By 1900, their films were extended scenes of up to 5 minutes long.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Most films of this period were what came to be called "chase films". These were inspired by
       <a href="/wiki/James_Williamson_(film_pioneer)" title="James Williamson (film pioneer)">
        James Williamson
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Stop_Thief!" title="Stop Thief!">
         Stop Thief!
        </a>
       </i>
       of 1901, which showed a tramp stealing a leg of mutton from a butcher's boy in the first shot, then being chased through the second shot by the butcher's boy and assorted dogs, and finally being caught by the dogs in the third shot. Several British films made in the first half of 1903 extended the chase method of film construction. These included
       <i>
        An Elopement à la Mode
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        The Pickpocket: A Chase Through London
       </i>
       , made by Alf Collins for the British branch of the French
       <a href="/wiki/Gaumont_Film_Company" title="Gaumont Film Company">
        Gaumont company
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        Daring Daylight Burglary
       </i>
       , made by
       <a href="/wiki/Frank_Mottershaw" title="Frank Mottershaw">
        Frank Mottershaw
       </a>
       at the Sheffield Photographic Company, and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Desperate_Poaching_Affray" title="Desperate Poaching Affray">
         Desperate Poaching Affray
        </a>
       </i>
       , made by
       <a href="/wiki/William_Haggar" title="William Haggar">
        William Haggar
       </a>
       . Haggar in particular innovated the first extant panning shots; the
       <a href="/wiki/Poaching" title="Poaching">
        poachers
       </a>
       are chased by gamekeepers and police officers and the camera pans along, creating a sense of urgency and speed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Affray_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Affray-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       His films were also recognised for their intelligent use of depth of staging and screen edges, while film academic
       <a href="/wiki/No%C3%ABl_Burch" title="Noël Burch">
        Noël Burch
       </a>
       praised Haggar's effective use of off-screen space.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BFI_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-BFI-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       He was also one of the first film makers to purposefully introduce violence for entertainment; in
       <i>
        Desperate Poaching Affray
       </i>
       the villains are seen firing guns at their pursuers.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Great_Train_Robbery_0018.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="576" data-file-width="720" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/The_Great_Train_Robbery_0018.jpg/220px-The_Great_Train_Robbery_0018.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/The_Great_Train_Robbery_0018.jpg/330px-The_Great_Train_Robbery_0018.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/The_Great_Train_Robbery_0018.jpg/440px-The_Great_Train_Robbery_0018.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Great_Train_Robbery_0018.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Still from
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/The_Great_Train_Robbery_(1903_film)" title="The Great Train Robbery (1903 film)">
           The Great Train Robbery
          </a>
         </i>
         , produced by
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edwin_Stanton_Porter" title="Edwin Stanton Porter">
          Edwin S. Porter
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Other filmmakers took up all these ideas including the American
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edwin_Stanton_Porter" title="Edwin Stanton Porter">
        Edwin S. Porter
       </a>
       , who started making films for the Edison Company in 1901. Porter, a projectionist, was hired by Thomas Edison to develop his new projection model known as the
       <a href="/wiki/Vitascope" title="Vitascope">
        Vitascope
       </a>
       . Porter wanted to develop a style of filmmaking that would move away from the one-shot short films into a "story-telling [narrative]" style.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gazetas,_Aristides_2000_12-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gazetas,_Aristides_2000-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       When he began making longer films in 1902, he put a dissolve between every shot, just as Georges Méliès was already doing, and he frequently had the same action repeated across the dissolves. His film,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Great_Train_Robbery_(1903_film)" title="The Great Train Robbery (1903 film)">
         The Great Train Robbery
        </a>
       </i>
       (1903), had a running time of twelve minutes, with twenty separate shots and ten different indoor and outdoor locations. He used
       <a href="/wiki/Cross-cutting" title="Cross-cutting">
        cross-cutting
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Film_editing" title="Film editing">
        editing
       </a>
       method to show simultaneous action in different places. The time continuity in
       <i>
        The Great Train Robbery
       </i>
       was actually more confusing than that in the films it was modeled on, but nevertheless it was a greater success than them due to its
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Old_West" title="American Old West">
        Wild West
       </a>
       violence.
       <i>
        The Great Train Robbery
       </i>
       served as one of the vehicles that would launch the film medium into mass popularity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gazetas,_Aristides_2000_12-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gazetas,_Aristides_2000-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Pathé company in France also made imitations and variations of Smith and Williamson's films from 1902 onwards using cuts between the shots, which helped to standardize the basics of film construction. An influential French film of the period was Méliès's 14-minute-long
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/A_Trip_to_the_Moon" title="A Trip to the Moon">
         A Trip to the Moon
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammond_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hammond-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was extremely popular at the time of its release, and is the best-known of the hundreds of films made by Méliès. It was one of the first known
       <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction_film" title="Science fiction film">
        science fiction films
       </a>
       , and used innovative
       <a href="/wiki/Animation" title="Animation">
        animation
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Special_effects" title="Special effects">
        special effects
       </a>
       , including the well-known image of the spaceship landing in the Moon's eye. The sheer volume of Pathé's production led to their filmmakers giving a further precision and polish to the details of film continuity.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Animation">
        Animation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Animation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The first use of animation in movies was in 1899, with the production of the short film Matches: An Appeal by British film pioneer
       <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Melbourne-Cooper" title="Arthur Melbourne-Cooper">
        Arthur Melbourne-Cooper
       </a>
       - a thirty-second long stop-motion animated piece intended to encourage the audience to send matches to British troops fighting the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boer_War" title="Boer War">
        Boer War
       </a>
       . The film contains an appeal to send money to
       <a href="/wiki/Bryant_and_May" title="Bryant and May">
        Bryant and May
       </a>
       who would then send matches to the troops fighting in South Africa. It was shown in December 1899 at The Empire Theatre in London. This film is the earliest known example of stop-motion animation. Little puppets, constructed of matchsticks, are writing the appeal on a black wall. Their movements are filmed frame by frame, movement by movement.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Humorous_Phases_of_Funny_Faces_screenshot.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="716" data-file-width="950" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Humorous_Phases_of_Funny_Faces_screenshot.jpg/220px-Humorous_Phases_of_Funny_Faces_screenshot.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Humorous_Phases_of_Funny_Faces_screenshot.jpg/330px-Humorous_Phases_of_Funny_Faces_screenshot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Humorous_Phases_of_Funny_Faces_screenshot.jpg/440px-Humorous_Phases_of_Funny_Faces_screenshot.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Humorous_Phases_of_Funny_Faces_screenshot.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A single frame from the
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Humorous_Phases_of_Funny_Faces" title="Humorous Phases of Funny Faces">
           Humorous Phases of Funny Faces
          </a>
         </i>
         animation, showing the use of
         <a href="/wiki/Cutout_animation" title="Cutout animation">
          cut-out
         </a>
         technique
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The relative sophistication of this piece was not followed up for some time, with subsequent works in animation being limited to short, two or three frame effects, such as appeared in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edwin_Stanton_Porter" title="Edwin Stanton Porter">
        Edwin Stanton Porter
       </a>
       's 1902 short
       <i>
        Fun in a Bakery Shop
       </i>
       , where a lump of dough was made to smile over the course of a three-frame sequence. Works rivaling the British short in length did not appear until 1905, when Edwin Porter made
       <i>
        How Jones Lost His Roll
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        The Whole Dam Family and the Dam Dog
       </i>
       . Both of these films had intertitles which were formed by the letters moving into place from a random scattering to form the words of the titles. This was done by exposing the film one frame at a time, and moving the letters a little bit towards their final position between each exposure. This is what has come to be called "single frame
       <a href="/wiki/Animation" title="Animation">
        animation
       </a>
       " or "object animation", and it needs a slightly adapted camera that exposes only one frame for each turn of the crank handle, rather than the usual eight frames per turn.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dartagnan-musketeers.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="700" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Dartagnan-musketeers.jpg/210px-Dartagnan-musketeers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Dartagnan-musketeers.jpg/315px-Dartagnan-musketeers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Dartagnan-musketeers.jpg/420px-Dartagnan-musketeers.jpg 2x" width="210"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dartagnan-musketeers.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Lubitsch" title="Ernst Lubitsch">
          Ernst Lubitsch
         </a>
         known as the master of kostumfilme
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In 1906,
       <a href="/wiki/Albert_E._Smith_(producer)" title="Albert E. Smith (producer)">
        Albert Edward Smith
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/J._Stuart_Blackton" title="J. Stuart Blackton">
        James Stuart Blackton
       </a>
       at
       <a href="/wiki/Vitagraph_Studios" title="Vitagraph Studios">
        Vitagraph Studios
       </a>
       took the next step, and in their
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Humorous_Phases_of_Funny_Faces" title="Humorous Phases of Funny Faces">
         Humorous Phases of Funny Faces
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beckerman2003_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Beckerman2003-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       what appear to be cartoon drawings of people move from one pose to another. This is done for most of the length of this film by moving jointed cut-outs of the figures frame by frame between the exposures, just as Porter moved his letters. However, there is a very short section of the film where things are made to appear to move by altering the drawings themselves from frame to frame, which is how
       <a href="/wiki/Traditional_animation" title="Traditional animation">
        standard animated cartoons
       </a>
       have since been made up to today.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Quirino_Cristiani_con_una_figura.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="485" data-file-width="288" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Quirino_Cristiani_con_una_figura.jpg/150px-Quirino_Cristiani_con_una_figura.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Quirino_Cristiani_con_una_figura.jpg/225px-Quirino_Cristiani_con_una_figura.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6b/Quirino_Cristiani_con_una_figura.jpg 2x" width="150"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Quirino_Cristiani_con_una_figura.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/El_Ap%C3%B3stol" title="El Apóstol">
           El Apóstol
          </a>
         </i>
         , the world's first animated feature film, and
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Pelud%C3%B3polis" title="Peludópolis">
           Peludópolis
          </a>
         </i>
         , the first animated feature film with sound, were produced in cutout animation by Italian-born cartoonist
         <a href="/wiki/Quirino_Cristiani" title="Quirino Cristiani">
          Quirino Cristiani
         </a>
         .
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBendazzi199449_23-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBendazzi199449-23">
           [23]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The technique of single frame animation was further developed in 1907 by Edwin S. Porter in
       <i>
        The Teddy Bears
       </i>
       and by J. Stuart Blackton with
       <i>
        Work Made Easy
       </i>
       . In the first of these the toy bears were made to move, apparently on their own, and in the latter film building tools were made to perform construction tasks without human intervention, by using frame-by-frame animation. The technique got to Europe almost immediately, and Segundo de Chomon and others at Pathé took it further, adding
       <a href="/wiki/Clay_animation" title="Clay animation">
        clay animation
       </a>
       , in which sculptures were deformed from one thing into another thing frame by frame in
       <i>
        Sculpture moderne
       </i>
       (1908), and then Pathé made the next step to the animation of silhouette shapes. Also in France,
       <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Cohl" title="Émile Cohl">
        Émile Cohl
       </a>
       fully developed drawn animation in a series of films starting with
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Fantasmagorie_(1908_film)" title="Fantasmagorie (1908 film)">
         Fantasmagorie
        </a>
       </i>
       (1908), in which humans and objects drawn as outline figures went though a series of remarkable interactions and transformations. In the United States the response was from the famous strip
       <a href="/wiki/Cartoonist" title="Cartoonist">
        cartoon artist
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Winsor_McCay" title="Winsor McCay">
        Winsor McCay
       </a>
       , who drew much more realistic animated figures going through smoother, more naturalistic motion in a series of films starting with the film
       <i>
        Little Nemo
       </i>
       , made for Vitagraph in 1911. In the next few years various others took part in this development of
       <a href="/wiki/Animated_cartoon" title="Animated cartoon">
        animated cartoons
       </a>
       in the United States and elsewhere.
      </p>
      <p>
       The world's first animated feature film was
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/El_Ap%C3%B3stol" title="El Apóstol">
         El Apóstol
        </a>
       </i>
       (1917), made by Italian-Argentine cartoonist
       <a href="/wiki/Quirino_Cristiani" title="Quirino Cristiani">
        Quirino Cristiani
       </a>
       utilizing cutout animation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinkielman2004_24-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinkielman2004-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrafton1993378_25-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrafton1993378-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Cristiani also directed the first animated feature film with sound,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Pelud%C3%B3polis" title="Peludópolis">
         Peludópolis
        </a>
       </i>
       , released with a vitaphone sound-on-disc synchronization system soundtrack. Unfortunately, a fire that destroyed producer Federico Valle's film studio incinerated the only known copies of the movies, and they are now considered as lost films.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckerman200325_26-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckerman200325-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBendazzi1996_27-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBendazzi1996-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1932, the first short animated film created entirely with
       <a href="/wiki/Technicolor" title="Technicolor">
        technicolor
       </a>
       (the trichromatic procedure (green, red, blue), whose use required a triple photographic impression, incorporation of chromatic filters and cameras of enormous dimensions) was
       <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney" title="Walt Disney">
        Walt Disney
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Flowers_and_Trees" title="Flowers and Trees">
         Flowers and Trees
        </a>
       </i>
       , directed by
       <a href="/wiki/Burt_Gillett" title="Burt Gillett">
        Burt Gillett
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esquire1_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-esquire1-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Feature_film">
        Feature film
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Feature film">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Feature_film" title="Feature film">
        Feature film
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hollywood_Sign_(Zuschnitt).jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6916" data-file-width="11065" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Hollywood_Sign_%28Zuschnitt%29.jpg/250px-Hollywood_Sign_%28Zuschnitt%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Hollywood_Sign_%28Zuschnitt%29.jpg/375px-Hollywood_Sign_%28Zuschnitt%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Hollywood_Sign_%28Zuschnitt%29.jpg/500px-Hollywood_Sign_%28Zuschnitt%29.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hollywood_Sign_(Zuschnitt).jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Hollywood_Sign" title="Hollywood Sign">
          Hollywood Sign
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Films at the time were no longer than one reel, although some multi-reel films had been made on the life of Christ in the first few years of cinema. The first feature length multi-reel film in the world was the 1906 Australian production called
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Story_of_the_Kelly_Gang" title="The Story of the Kelly Gang">
         The Story of the Kelly Gang
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       It traced the life of the legendary infamous
       <a href="/wiki/Outlaw" title="Outlaw">
        outlaw
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Bushranger" title="Bushranger">
        bushranger
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Ned_Kelly" title="Ned Kelly">
        Ned Kelly
       </a>
       (1855–1880) and ran for more than an hour with a reel length of approximately 4,000 feet (1,200 m).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R&amp;P_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-R&amp;P-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was first shown at the
       <a href="/wiki/Melbourne_Athenaeum" title="Melbourne Athenaeum">
        Athenaeum Hall
       </a>
       in Collins Street, Melbourne, Australia on 26 December 1906 and in the UK in January 1908.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cinema_32-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-cinema-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Maturation">
        Maturation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Maturation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Film_business">
        Film business
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Film business">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Biograph_poster2.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3942" data-file-width="2668" height="325" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Biograph_poster2.jpg/220px-Biograph_poster2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Biograph_poster2.jpg/330px-Biograph_poster2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Biograph_poster2.jpg/440px-Biograph_poster2.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Biograph_poster2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Poster for a
         <a href="/wiki/Biograph_Studios" title="Biograph Studios">
          Biograph Studios
         </a>
         release from 1913.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The first successful permanent theatre showing only films was "The Nickelodeon", which was opened in Pittsburgh in 1905. By then there were enough films several minutes long available to fill a programme running for at least half an hour, and which could be changed weekly when the local audience became bored with it. Other exhibitors in the United States quickly followed suit, and within a couple of years there were thousands of these
       <a href="/wiki/Nickelodeon_(movie_theater)" title="Nickelodeon (movie theater)">
        nickelodeons
       </a>
       in operation. The American experience led to a worldwide boom in the production and exhibition of films from 1906 onwards.
      </p>
      <p>
       By 1907 purpose-built cinemas for motion pictures were being opened across the United States, Britain and France. The films were often shown with the accompaniment of music provided by a pianist, though there could be more musicians. There were also a very few larger cinemas in some of the biggest cities. Initially, the majority of films in the programmes were
       <a href="/wiki/Path%C3%A9" title="Pathé">
        Pathé
       </a>
       films, but this changed fairly quickly as the American companies cranked up production. The programme was made up of just a few films, and the show lasted around 30 minutes. The reel of film, of maximum length 1,000 feet (300 m), which usually contained one individual film, became the standard unit of film production and exhibition in this period. The programme was changed twice or more a week, but went up to five changes of programme a week after a couple of years. In general, cinemas were set up in the established entertainment districts of the cities. In 1907, Pathé began renting their films to cinemas through film exchanges rather than selling the films outright.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:320px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Battle_of_Chemulpo_Bay_edison.ogv/320px--Battle_of_Chemulpo_Bay_edison.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:320px;height:240px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="70.8708" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Battle_of_Chemulpo_Bay_edison.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/8/86/Battle_of_Chemulpo_Bay_edison.ogv/Battle_of_Chemulpo_Bay_edison.ogv.240p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Small WebM (240P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 240P" data-transcodekey="240p.webm" data-width="320" data-height="240" data-bandwidth="250904" data-framerate="29.97002997003"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/86/Battle_of_Chemulpo_Bay_edison.ogv" type="video/ogg; codecs=&amp;quot;theora&amp;quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file, 320 × 240 (1.16 Mbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="320" data-height="240" data-bandwidth="1162869" data-framerate="29.97002997003"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/8/86/Battle_of_Chemulpo_Bay_edison.ogv/Battle_of_Chemulpo_Bay_edison.ogv.160p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Low bandwidth WebM (160P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 160P" data-transcodekey="160p.webm" data-width="214" data-height="160" data-bandwidth="128240" data-framerate="29.97002997003"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;'>
         <img alt="File:Battle of Chemulpo Bay edison.ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Battle_of_Chemulpo_Bay_edison.ogv/220px--Battle_of_Chemulpo_Bay_edison.ogv.jpg" style="width:220px;height:165px"/>
         <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/86/Battle_of_Chemulpo_Bay_edison.ogv" target="new" title="Play media">
          <span class="play-btn-large">
           <span class="mw-tmh-playtext">
            Play media
           </span>
          </span>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Chemulpo_Bay_edison.ogv" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         An early film, depicting a re-enactment of the
         <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Chemulpo_Bay" title="Battle of Chemulpo Bay">
          Battle of Chemulpo Bay
         </a>
         (Film produced in 1904 by
         <a href="/wiki/Edison_Studios" title="Edison Studios">
          Edison Studios
         </a>
         )
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       By about 1910, actors began to receive screen credit for their roles, and the way to the creation of film stars was opened. Films were increasingly longer, and began to feature proper plots and development.
      </p>
      <p>
       The litigation over patents between all the major American film-making companies led to the formation of a trust to control the American film business, with each company in the trust being allocated production quotas (two reels a week for the biggest ones, one reel a week for the smaller). However, although 6,000 exhibitors signed up to the trust, about 2,000 others did not and began to fund new film producing companies. By 1912 the independents had nearly half of the market and the government defeated the trust by initiating
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antitrust" title="Antitrust">
        anti-trust
       </a>
       action at the same time.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the early 20th century, before
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hollywood,_Los_Angeles" title="Hollywood, Los Angeles">
        Hollywood
       </a>
       , the motion picture industry was based in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/America%27s_first_motion_picture_industry" title="America's first motion picture industry">
        Fort Lee, New Jersey
       </a>
       across the Hudson River from New York City.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In need of a winter headquarters, moviemakers were attracted to
       <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville,_Florida" title="Jacksonville, Florida">
        Jacksonville, Florida
       </a>
       due to its warm climate, exotic locations, excellent rail access, and cheaper labor, earning the city the title of "The Winter Film Capital of the World." New York-based
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kalem_Studios" title="Kalem Studios">
        Kalem Studios
       </a>
       was the first to open a permanent studio in Jacksonville in 1908. Over the course of the next decade, more than 30 silent film companies established studios in town, including
       <a href="/wiki/Metro_Pictures" title="Metro Pictures">
        Metro Pictures
       </a>
       (later
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MGM" title="MGM">
        MGM
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Edison_Studios" title="Edison Studios">
        Edison Studios
       </a>
