       , Majestic Films, King Bee Film Company,
       <a href="/wiki/Vim_Comedy_Company" title="Vim Comedy Company">
        Vim Comedy Company
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Norman_Studios" title="Norman Studios">
        Norman Studios
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaumont_Studios" title="Gaumont Studios">
        Gaumont Studios
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Lubin_Manufacturing_Company" title="Lubin Manufacturing Company">
        Lubin Manufacturing Company
       </a>
       . Comedic actor and Georgia native
       <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Hardy" title="Oliver Hardy">
        Oliver "Babe" Hardy
       </a>
       began his motion picture career here in 1914. He starred in over 36 short silent films his first year acting. With the closing of Lubin in early 1915, Oliver moved to New York then New Jersey to find film jobs. Acquiring a job with the Vim Company in early 1915, he returned to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jacksonville" title="Jacksonville">
        Jacksonville
       </a>
       in the spring of 1917 before relocating to Los Angeles in October 1917. The first motion picture made in
       <a href="/wiki/Technicolor" title="Technicolor">
        Technicolor
       </a>
       and the first feature-length color movie produced in the United States,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Gulf_Between" title="The Gulf Between">
        The Gulf Between
       </a>
       , was also filmed on location in Jacksonville in 1917.
      </p>
      <p>
       Jacksonville was especially important to the
       <a href="/wiki/African-American_art" title="African-American art">
        African American film
       </a>
       industry. One notable individual in this regard is the European American producer Richard Norman, who created a string of films starring black actors in the vein of
       <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Micheaux" title="Oscar Micheaux">
        Oscar Micheaux
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Motion_Picture_Company" title="Lincoln Motion Picture Company">
        Lincoln Motion Picture Company
       </a>
       . In contrast to the degrading parts offered in certain white films such as
       <a href="/wiki/The_Birth_of_a_Nation" title="The Birth of a Nation">
        The Birth of a Nation
       </a>
       , Norman and his contemporaries sought to create positive stories featuring African Americans in what he termed "splendidly assuming different roles."
      </p>
      <p>
       Jacksonville's mostly conservative residents, however, objected to the hallmarks of the early movie industry, such as car chases in the streets, simulated bank robberies and fire alarms in public places, and even the occasional riot. In 1917, conservative Democrat
       <a href="/wiki/John_W._Martin" title="John W. Martin">
        John W. Martin
       </a>
       was elected mayor on the platform of taming the city's movie industry. By that time, southern
       <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">
        California
       </a>
       was emerging as the major movie production center, thanks in large part to the move of film pioneers like
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Selig" title="William Selig">
        William Selig
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D.W._Griffith" title="D.W. Griffith">
        D.W. Griffith
       </a>
       to the area. These factors quickly sealed the demise of Jacksonville as a major film destination.
      </p>
      <p>
       Another factor for the industry's move west was that up until 1913, most American film production was still carried out around New York, but due to the monopoly of
       <a href="/wiki/Thomas_A._Edison,_Inc." title="Thomas A. Edison, Inc.">
        Thomas A. Edison, Inc.
       </a>
       's film patents and its litigious attempts to preserve it, many filmmakers moved to Southern California, starting with
       <a href="/wiki/William_Nicholas_Selig" title="William Nicholas Selig">
        Selig
       </a>
       in 1909. The sunshine and scenery was important for the production of Westerns, which came to form a major American film genre with the first cowboy stars,
       <a href="/wiki/Broncho_Billy_Anderson" title="Broncho Billy Anderson">
        G.M. Anderson ("Broncho Billy")
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Tom_Mix" title="Tom Mix">
        Tom Mix
       </a>
       . Selig pioneered the use of (fairly) wild animals from a zoo for a series of exotic adventures, with the actors being menaced or saved by the animals.
       <a href="/wiki/Kalem_Company" title="Kalem Company">
        Kalem Company
       </a>
       sent film crews to places in America and abroad to film stories in the actual places they were supposed to have happened. Kalem also pioneered the female action heroine from 1912, with
       <a href="/wiki/Ruth_Roland" title="Ruth Roland">
        Ruth Roland
       </a>
       playing starring roles in their Westerns.
      </p>
      <p>
       In France, Pathé retained its dominant position, followed still by Gaumont, and then other new companies that appeared to cater to the film boom. A film company with a different approach was Film d'Art. This was set up at the beginning of 1908 to make films of a serious artistic nature. Their declared programme was to make films using only the best dramatists, artists and actors. The first of these was
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Assassination_of_the_Duke_of_Guise" title="The Assassination of the Duke of Guise">
         L'Assassinat du Duc de Guise
        </a>
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        The Assassination of the Duc de Guise
       </i>
       ), a historical subject set in the court of
       <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_France" title="Henry III of France">
        Henri III
       </a>
       . This film used leading actors from the
       <a href="/wiki/Com%C3%A9die-Fran%C3%A7aise" title="Comédie-Française">
        Comédie-Française
       </a>
       , and had a special accompanying score written by
       <a href="/wiki/Camille_Saint-Sa%C3%ABns" title="Camille Saint-Saëns">
        Camille Saint-Saëns
       </a>
       . The other French majors followed suit, and this wave gave rise to the English-language description of films with artistic pretensions aimed at a sophisticated audience as
       <a href="/wiki/Art_film" title="Art film">
        "art films"
       </a>
       . By 1910, the French film companies were starting to make films as long as two, or even three reels, though most were still one reel long. This trend was followed in Italy, Denmark, and Sweden.
      </p>
      <p>
       In Britain, the
       <a href="/wiki/Cinematograph_Act_1909" title="Cinematograph Act 1909">
        Cinematograph Act 1909
       </a>
       was the first
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primary_legislation" title="Primary legislation">
        primary legislation
       </a>
       to specifically regulate the
       <a href="/wiki/Film_industry" title="Film industry">
        film industry
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Cinematograph" title="Cinematograph">
        Film exhibitions
       </a>
       often took place in temporary venues and the use of highly flammable
       <a href="/wiki/Nitrocellulose" title="Nitrocellulose">
        cellulose nitrate
       </a>
       for film, combined with
       <a href="/wiki/Limelight" title="Limelight">
        limelight
       </a>
       illumination, created a significant fire hazard. The Act specified a strict building code which required, amongst other things, that the projector be enclosed within a fire resisting enclosure.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Regular newsreels were exhibited from 1910 and soon became a popular way for finding out the news –  the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Antarctic_Expedition,_1910%E2%80%9313" title="British Antarctic Expedition, 1910–13">
        British Antarctic Expedition
       </a>
       to the
       <a href="/wiki/South_Pole" title="South Pole">
        South Pole
       </a>
       was filmed for the newsreels as were the
       <a href="/wiki/Suffragette" title="Suffragette">
        suffragette
       </a>
       demonstrations that were happening at the same time.
       <a href="/wiki/F._Percy_Smith" title="F. Percy Smith">
        F. Percy Smith
       </a>
       was an early
       <a href="/wiki/Nature_documentary" title="Nature documentary">
        nature documentary
       </a>
       pioneer working for
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_Urban" title="Charles Urban">
        Charles Urban
       </a>
       and he pioneered the use of time lapse and micro cinematography in his 1910 documentary on the growth of flowers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BFIso0001_37-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-BFIso0001-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WFH01_38-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-WFH01-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="New_film_producing_countries">
        New film producing countries
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: New film producing countries">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       With the worldwide film boom, yet more countries now joined Britain, France, Germany and the United States in serious film production. In Italy, production was spread over several centres, with Turin being the first and biggest. There,
       <a href="/wiki/Ambrosio_Film" title="Ambrosio Film">
        Ambrosio
       </a>
       was the first company in the field in 1905, and remained the largest in the country through this period. Its most substantial rival was
       <a href="/wiki/Cines" title="Cines">
        Cines
       </a>
       in Rome, which started producing in 1906. The great strength of the
       <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Italy" title="Cinema of Italy">
        Italian industry
       </a>
       was historical epics, with large casts and massive scenery. As early as 1911,
       <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Pastrone" title="Giovanni Pastrone">
        Giovanni Pastrone
       </a>
       's two-reel
       <i>
        La Caduta di Troia (
        <a href="/wiki/The_Fall_of_Troy_(film)" title="The Fall of Troy (film)">
         The Fall of Troy
        </a>
        )
       </i>
       made a big impression worldwide, and it was followed by even bigger spectacles like
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quo_Vadis%3F" title="Quo Vadis?">
         Quo Vadis?
        </a>
       </i>
       (1912), which ran for 90 minutes, and Pastrone's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Cabiria" title="Cabiria">
         Cabiria
        </a>
       </i>
       of 1914, which ran for two and a half hours.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CadutaW1.gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="218" data-file-width="300" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5b/CadutaW1.gif" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CadutaW1.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          La Caduta di Troia (
          <a href="/wiki/The_Fall_of_Troy_(film)" title="The Fall of Troy (film)">
           The Fall of Troy
          </a>
          )
         </i>
         (1911).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Italian companies also had a strong line in slapstick comedy, with actors like
       <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Deed" title="André Deed">
        André Deed
       </a>
       , known locally as "Cretinetti", and elsewhere as "Foolshead" and "Gribouille", achieving worldwide fame with his almost surrealistic gags.
      </p>
      <p>
       The most important film-producing country in Northern Europe up until the First World War was Denmark. The
       <a href="/wiki/Nordisk_Film" title="Nordisk Film">
        Nordisk
       </a>
       company was set up there in 1906 by
       <a href="/wiki/Ole_Olsen_(filmmaker)" title="Ole Olsen (filmmaker)">
        Ole Olsen
       </a>
       , a fairground showman, and after a brief period imitating the successes of French and British filmmakers, in 1907 he produced 67 films, most directed by Viggo Larsen, with sensational subjects like
       <i>
        Den hvide Slavinde (The White Slave)
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Isbjørnenjagt (Polar Bear Hunt)
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Løvejagten (The Lion Hunt)
       </i>
       . By 1910, new smaller Danish companies began joining the business, and besides making more films about the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_Slavery_(prostitution)" title="White Slavery (prostitution)">
        white slave trade
       </a>
       , they contributed other new subjects. The most important of these finds was
       <a href="/wiki/Asta_Nielsen" title="Asta Nielsen">
        Asta Nielsen
       </a>
       in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Abyss_(1910_film)" title="The Abyss (1910 film)">
         Afgrunden
        </a>
        (The Abyss)
       </i>
       , directed by
       <a href="/wiki/Urban_Gad" title="Urban Gad">
        Urban Gad
       </a>
       for Kosmorama, This combined the circus, sex, jealousy and murder, all put over with great conviction, and pushed the other Danish filmmakers further in this direction. By 1912, the Danish film companies were multiplying rapidly.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Sweden" title="Cinema of Sweden">
        Swedish film industry
       </a>
       was smaller and slower to get started than the Danish industry. Here, the important man was
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_Magnusson" title="Charles Magnusson">
        Charles Magnusson
       </a>
       , a newsreel cameraman for the Svenskabiografteatern cinema chain. He started fiction film production for them in 1909, directing a number of the films himself. Production increased in 1912, when the company engaged
       <a href="/wiki/Victor_Sj%C3%B6str%C3%B6m" title="Victor Sjöström">
        Victor Sjöström
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Mauritz_Stiller" title="Mauritz Stiller">
        Mauritz Stiller
       </a>
       as directors. They started out by imitating the subjects favoured by the Danish film industry, but by 1913 they were producing their own strikingly original work, which sold very well.
      </p>
      <p>
       Russia began its film industry in 1908 with Pathé shooting some fiction subjects there, and then the creation of real Russian film companies by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aleksandr_Drankov" title="Aleksandr Drankov">
        Aleksandr Drankov
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Aleksandr_Khanzhonkov" title="Aleksandr Khanzhonkov">
        Aleksandr Khanzhonkov
       </a>
       . The Khanzhonkov company quickly became much the largest Russian film company, and remained so until 1918.
      </p>
      <p>
       In Germany,
       <a href="/wiki/Oskar_Messter" title="Oskar Messter">
        Oskar Messter
       </a>
       had been involved in film-making from 1896, but did not make a significant number of films per year till 1910. When the worldwide film boom started, he, and the few other people in the German film business, continued to sell prints of their own films outright, which put them at a disadvantage. It was only when
       <a href="/wiki/Paul_Davidson_(producer)" title="Paul Davidson (producer)">
        Paul Davidson
       </a>
       , the owner of a chain of cinemas, brought Asta Nielsen and Urban Gad to Germany from Denmark in 1911, and set up a production company, Projektions-AG "Union" (
       <a href="/wiki/PAGU" title="PAGU">
        PAGU
       </a>
       ), for them, that a change-over to renting prints began. Messter replied with a series of longer films starring Henny Porten, but although these did well in the German-speaking world, they were not particularly successful internationally, unlike the Asta Nielsen films. Another of the growing German film producers just before World War I was the German branch of the French
       <a href="/wiki/Eclair_(company)" title="Eclair (company)">
        Éclair
       </a>
       company, Deutsche Éclair. This was expropriated by the German government, and turned into DECLA when the war started. But altogether, German producers only had a minor part of the German market in 1914.
      </p>
      <p>
       Overall, from about 1910, American films had the largest share of the market in all European countries except France, and even in France, the American films had just pushed the local production out of first place on the eve of World War I. So even if the war had not happened, American films may have become dominant worldwide. Although the war made things much worse for European producers, the technical qualities of American films made them increasingly attractive to audiences everywhere.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Film_technique">
        Film technique
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Film technique">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:VitstuW1.gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="317" data-file-width="400" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b5/VitstuW1.gif/220px-VitstuW1.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b5/VitstuW1.gif/330px-VitstuW1.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/b/b5/VitstuW1.gif 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:VitstuW1.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A.E._Smith" title="A.E. Smith">
          A.E. Smith
         </a>
         filming
         <i>
          The Bargain Fiend
         </i>
         in the
         <a href="/wiki/Vitagraph_Studios" title="Vitagraph Studios">
          Vitagraph Studios
         </a>
         in 1907. Arc floodlights hang overhead.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       New film techniques that were introduced in this period include the use of artificial lighting, fire effects and
       <a href="/wiki/Low-key_lighting" title="Low-key lighting">
        Low-key lighting
       </a>
       (i.e. lighting in which most of the frame is dark) for enhanced atmosphere during sinister scenes.
      </p>
      <p>
       Continuity of action from shot to shot was also refined, such as in Pathé's
       <i>
        le Cheval emballé (The Runaway Horse)
       </i>
       (1907) where
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cross_cutting" title="Cross cutting">
        cross-cutting between parallel actions
       </a>
       is used.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_Wark_Griffith" title="David Wark Griffith">
        D. W. Griffith
       </a>
       also began using cross-cutting in the film
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Fatal_Hour_(1908_film)" title="The Fatal Hour (1908 film)">
         The Fatal Hour
        </a>
       </i>
       , made in July 1908. Another development was the use of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Point_of_view_shot" title="Point of view shot">
        Point of View shot
       </a>
       , first used in 1910 in Vitagraph's
       <i>
        Back to Nature
       </i>
       . Insert shots were also used for artistic purposes; the Italian film
       <i>
        La mala planta (The Evil Plant)
       </i>
       , directed by
       <a href="/wiki/Mario_Caserini" title="Mario Caserini">
        Mario Caserini
       </a>
       had an insert shot of a snake slithering over the "Evil Plant".
      </p>
      <p>
       As films grew longer, specialist writers were employed to simplify more complex stories derived from novels or plays into a form that could be contained on one reel.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genres" title="Genres">
        Genres
       </a>
       began to be used as categories; the main division was into comedy and drama, but these categories were further subdivided.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intertitles" title="Intertitles">
        Intertitles
       </a>
       containing lines of dialogue began to be used consistently from 1908 onwards, such as in Vitagraph's
       <i>
        An Auto Heroine; or, The Race for the Vitagraph Cup and How It Was Won
       </i>
       . The dialogue was eventually inserted into the middle of the scene and became commonplace by 1912. The introduction of dialogue titles transformed the nature of film narrative. When dialogue titles came to be always cut into a scene just after a character starts speaking, and then left with a cut to the character just before they finish speaking, then one had something that was effectively the equivalent of a present-day sound film.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="During_World_War_I">
        During World War I
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: During World War I">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Industry">
        Industry
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Industry">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CABINET_DES_DR_CALIGARI_01.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="210" data-file-width="300" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/CABINET_DES_DR_CALIGARI_01.jpg/250px-CABINET_DES_DR_CALIGARI_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/f/fd/CABINET_DES_DR_CALIGARI_01.jpg 1.5x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CABINET_DES_DR_CALIGARI_01.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The visual style of
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/The_Cabinet_of_Dr._Caligari" title="The Cabinet of Dr. Caligari">
           The Cabinet of Dr. Caligari
          </a>
         </i>
         included deliberately distorted forms, and shadows and streaks of light painted directly onto the sets. It uses
         <a href="/wiki/Mise-en-sc%C3%A8ne" title="Mise-en-scène">
          Mise-en-scène
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The years of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_World_War" title="First World War">
        First World War
       </a>
       were a complex transitional period for the film industry. The exhibition of films changed from short one-reel programmes to feature films. Exhibition venues became larger and began charging higher prices.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the United States, these changes brought destruction to many film companies, the Vitagraph company being an exception. Film production began to shift to
       <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">
        Los Angeles
       </a>
       during World War I. The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universal_Studios" title="Universal Studios">
        Universal Film Manufacturing Company
       </a>
       was formed in 1912 as an umbrella company. New entrants included the
       <a href="/wiki/Jesse_L._Lasky" title="Jesse L. Lasky">
        Jesse Lasky
       </a>
       Feature Play Company, and Famous Players, both formed in 1913, and later amalgamated into
       <a href="/wiki/Famous_Players-Lasky" title="Famous Players-Lasky">
        Famous Players-Lasky
       </a>
       . The biggest success of these years was
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_Wark_Griffith" title="David Wark Griffith">
        David Wark Griffith
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Birth_of_a_Nation" title="The Birth of a Nation">
         The Birth of a Nation
        </a>
       </i>
       (1915). Griffith followed this up with the even bigger
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Intolerance_(film)" title="Intolerance (film)">
         Intolerance
        </a>
       </i>
       (1916), but, due to the high quality of film produced in the US, the market for their films was high.
      </p>
      <p>
       In France, film production shut down due to the general
       <a href="/wiki/Mobilization" title="Mobilization">
        military mobilization
       </a>
       of the country at the start of the war. Although film production began again in 1915, it was on a reduced scale, and the biggest companies gradually retired from production. Italian film production held up better, although so called "diva films", starring anguished female leads were a commercial failure. In Denmark, the Nordisk company increased its production so much in 1915 and 1916 that it could not sell all its films, which led to a very sharp decline in Danish production, and the end of Denmark's importance on the world film scene.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Germany" title="Cinema of Germany">
        German film industry
       </a>
       was seriously weakened by the war. The most important of the new film producers at the time was
       <a href="/wiki/Joe_May" title="Joe May">
        Joe May
       </a>
       , who made a series of thrillers and
       <a href="/wiki/Adventure_film" title="Adventure film">
        adventure films
       </a>
       through the war years, but
       <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Lubitsch" title="Ernst Lubitsch">
        Ernst Lubitsch
       </a>
       also came into prominence with a series of very successful comedies and dramas.
      </p>
      <p>
       Because of the large local market for films in Russia, the industry there was not harmed by the war at first, although the isolation of the country led many Russian films to develop peculiarly distinctive features. In 1919, after the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bolshevik_Revolution" title="Bolshevik Revolution">
        Bolshevik Revolution
       </a>
       , an exodus of talent from the country took place and film production was drastically curtailed.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="New_techniques">
        New techniques
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: New techniques">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oyster1.gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="222" data-file-width="300" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/1/16/Oyster1.gif" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oyster1.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Complex vignette shot in
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/The_Oyster_Princess" title="The Oyster Princess">
           die Austernprinzessin
          </a>
          (The Oyster Princess)
         </i>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       At this time, studios were blacked out to allow shooting to be unaffected by changing sunlight. This was replaced with floodlights and spotlights. The widespread adoption of irising-in and out to begin and end scenes caught on in this period. This is the revelation of a film shot in a circular mask, which gradually gets larger till it expands beyond the frame. Other shaped slits were used, including vertical and diagonal apertures.
      </p>
      <p>
       A new idea taken over from still photography was "
       <a href="/wiki/Soft_focus" title="Soft focus">
        soft focus
       </a>
       ". This began in 1915, with some shots being intentionally thrown out of focus for expressive effect, as in
       <a href="/wiki/Mary_Pickford" title="Mary Pickford">
        Mary Pickford
       </a>
       starrer
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Fanchon_the_Cricket" title="Fanchon the Cricket">
         Fanchon the Cricket
        </a>
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       It was during this period that camera effects intended to convey the subjective feelings of characters in a film really began to be established. These could now be done as Point of View (
       <a href="/wiki/Point-of-view_shot" title="Point-of-view shot">
        POV
       </a>
       ) shots, as in Sidney Drew's
       <i>
        The Story of the Glove
       </i>
       (1915), where a wobbly hand-held shot of a door and its keyhole represents the POV of a drunken man. The use of anamorphic (in the general sense of distorted shape) images first appears in these years with
       <a href="/wiki/Abel_Gance" title="Abel Gance">
        Abel Gance
       </a>
       directed
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/La_Folie_du_Docteur_Tube" title="La Folie du Docteur Tube">
         la Folie du Docteur Tube
        </a>
        (The Madness of Dr. Tube)
       </i>
       . In this film the effect of a drug administered to a group of people was suggested by shooting the scenes reflected in a
       <a href="/wiki/Curved_mirror" title="Curved mirror">
        distorting mirror
       </a>
       of the fair-ground type.
      </p>
      <p>
       Symbolic effects taken over from conventional literary and artistic tradition continued to make some appearances in films during these years. In D. W. Griffith's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Avenging_Conscience" title="The Avenging Conscience">
         The Avenging Conscience
        </a>
       </i>
       (1914), the title "The birth of the evil thought" precedes a series of three shots of the protagonist looking at a spider, and ants eating an insect. Symbolist art and literature from the turn of the century also had a more general effect on a small number of films made in Italy and Russia. The supine acceptance of death resulting from passion and forbidden longings was a major feature of this art, and states of delirium dwelt on at length were important as well.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DemillW1.gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="236" data-file-width="300" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/4/43/DemillW1.gif" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DemillW1.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Insert shot in
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Old_Wives_for_New" title="Old Wives for New">
           Old Wives for New
          </a>
         </i>
         (
         <a href="/wiki/Cecil_B._DeMille" title="Cecil B. DeMille">
          Cecil B. DeMille
         </a>
         , 1918)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The use of
       <a href="/wiki/Insert_(filmmaking)" title="Insert (filmmaking)">
        insert shots
       </a>
       , i.e. close-ups of objects other than faces, had already been established by the Brighton school, but were infrequently used before 1914. It is really only with Griffith's
       <i>
        The Avenging Conscience
       </i>
       that a new phase in the use of the Insert Shot starts. As well as the symbolic inserts already mentioned, the film also made extensive use of large numbers of Big Close Up shots of clutching hands and tapping feet as a means of emphasizing those parts of the body as indicators of psychological tension.
      </p>
      <p>
       Atmospheric inserts were developed in Europe in the late 1910s. This kind of shot is one in a scene which neither contains any of the characters in the story, nor is a Point of View shot seen by one of them. An early example is in
       <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Tourneur" title="Maurice Tourneur">
        Maurice Tourneur
       </a>
       directed
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Pride_of_the_Clan" title="The Pride of the Clan">
         The Pride of the Clan
        </a>
       </i>
       (1917), in which there is a series of shots of waves beating on a rocky shore to demonstrate the harsh lives of the fishing folk.
       <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Elvey" title="Maurice Elvey">
        Maurice Elvey
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Nelson_(1918_film)" title="Nelson (1918 film)">
         Nelson; The Story of England's Immortal Naval Hero
        </a>
       </i>
       (1919) has a symbolic sequence dissolving from a picture of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_II,_German_Emperor" title="William II, German Emperor">
        Kaiser Wilhelm II
       </a>
       to a peacock, and then to a battleship.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lev_Kuleshov.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="337" data-file-width="295" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/16/Lev_Kuleshov.jpg/150px-Lev_Kuleshov.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/16/Lev_Kuleshov.jpg/225px-Lev_Kuleshov.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/1/16/Lev_Kuleshov.jpg 2x" width="150"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lev_Kuleshov.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Lev_Kuleshov" title="Lev Kuleshov">
          Lev Kuleshov
         </a>
         the founders of the world's first film school, the Moscow Film School. Development of the style of film a principle known as the
         <a href="/wiki/Kuleshov_effect" title="Kuleshov effect">
          Kuleshov effect
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       By 1914, continuity cinema was the established mode of commercial cinema. One of the advanced continuity techniques involved an accurate and smooth transition from one shot to another. Cutting to
       <i>
        different
       </i>
       angles within a scene also became well-established as a technique for dissecting a scene into shots in American films. If the direction of the shot changes by more than ninety degrees, it is called a reverse-angle cutting. The leading figure in the full development of reverse-angle cutting was
       <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Ince" title="Ralph Ince">
        Ralph Ince
       </a>
       in his films, such as
       <i>
        The Right Girl
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        His Phantom Sweetheart
       </i>
      </p>
      <p>
       The use of
       <a href="/wiki/Flashback_(narrative)" title="Flashback (narrative)">
        flash-back
       </a>
       structures continued to develop in this period, with the usual way of entering and leaving a flash-back being through a dissolve. The Vitagraph company's
       <i>
        The Man That Might Have Been
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/William_J._Humphrey" title="William J. Humphrey">
        William J. Humphrey
       </a>
       , 1914), is even more complex, with a series of reveries and flash-backs that contrast the protagonist's real passage through life with what might have been, if his son had not died.
      </p>
      <p>
       After 1914,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cross_cutting" title="Cross cutting">
        cross cutting
       </a>
       between parallel actions came to be used –  more so in American films than in European ones. Cross-cutting was often used to get new effects of contrast, such as the cross-cut sequence in
       <a href="/wiki/Cecil_B._DeMille" title="Cecil B. DeMille">
        Cecil B. DeMille
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Whispering_Chorus" title="The Whispering Chorus">
         The Whispering Chorus
        </a>
       </i>
       (1918), in which a supposedly dead husband is having a liaison with a Chinese prostitute in an opium den, while simultaneously his unknowing wife is being remarried in church.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Film_art">
        Film art
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Film art">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:304px;max-width:304px">
        <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Metropolis-new-tower-of-babel.png">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="273" data-file-width="365" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/28/Metropolis-new-tower-of-babel.png/300px-Metropolis-new-tower-of-babel.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/2/28/Metropolis-new-tower-of-babel.png 1.5x" width="300"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          The New
          <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_Babel" title="Tower of Babel">
           Tower of Babel
          </a>
          , Fredersen's headquarters in
          <a href="/wiki/Metropolis_(1927_film)" title="Metropolis (1927 film)">
           Metropolis
          </a>
          .
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder_-_The_Tower_of_Babel_(Vienna)_-_Google_Art_Project_-_edited.jpg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="21952" data-file-width="30000" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder_-_The_Tower_of_Babel_%28Vienna%29_-_Google_Art_Project_-_edited.jpg/300px-Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder_-_The_Tower_of_Babel_%28Vienna%29_-_Google_Art_Project_-_edited.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder_-_The_Tower_of_Babel_%28Vienna%29_-_Google_Art_Project_-_edited.jpg/450px-Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder_-_The_Tower_of_Babel_%28Vienna%29_-_Google_Art_Project_-_edited.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder_-_The_Tower_of_Babel_%28Vienna%29_-_Google_Art_Project_-_edited.jpg/600px-Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder_-_The_Tower_of_Babel_%28Vienna%29_-_Google_Art_Project_-_edited.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          The Tower of Babel in Maria's recounting of the fable was modeled after
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Tower_of_Babel_(Brueghel)" title="The Tower of Babel (Brueghel)">
           this 1563 painting
          </a>
          by
          <a href="/wiki/Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder" title="Pieter Bruegel the Elder">
           Pieter Brueghel
          </a>
          .
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBukatman199762-3_39-0">
           <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBukatman199762-3-39">
            [39]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The general trend in the development of cinema, led from the United States, was towards using the newly developed specifically filmic devices for expression of the narrative content of film stories, and combining this with the standard dramatic structures already in use in commercial theatre.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_Wark_Griffith" title="David Wark Griffith">
        D. W. Griffith
       </a>
       had the highest standing amongst American directors in the industry, because of the dramatic excitement he conveyed to the audience through his films.
       <a href="/wiki/Cecil_B._DeMille" title="Cecil B. DeMille">
        Cecil B. DeMille
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Cheat_(1915_film)" title="The Cheat (1915 film)">
         The Cheat
        </a>
       </i>
       (1915), brought out the moral dilemmas facing their characters in a more subtle way than Griffith. DeMille was also in closer touch with the reality of contemporary American life.
       <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Tourneur" title="Maurice Tourneur">
        Maurice Tourneur
       </a>
       was also highly ranked for the pictorial beauties of his films, together with the subtlety of his handling of fantasy, while at the same time he was capable of getting greater
       <a href="/wiki/Naturalism_(literature)" title="Naturalism (literature)">
        naturalism
       </a>
       from his actors at appropriate moments, as in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/A_Girl%27s_Folly" title="A Girl's Folly">
         A Girl's Folly
        </a>
       </i>
       (1917).
      </p>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mr._%26_Mrs._Sidney_Drew" title="Mr. &amp; Mrs. Sidney Drew">
        Sidney Drew
       </a>
       was the leader in developing "polite comedy", while slapstick was refined by
       <a href="/wiki/Roscoe_Arbuckle" title="Roscoe Arbuckle">
        Fatty Arbuckle
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_Spencer_Chaplin" title="Charles Spencer Chaplin">
        Charles Chaplin
       </a>
       , who both started with
       <a href="/wiki/Mack_Sennett" title="Mack Sennett">
        Mack Sennett
       </a>
       's Keystone company. They reduced the usual frenetic pace of Sennett's films to give the audience a chance to appreciate the subtlety and finesse of their movement, and the cleverness of their gags. By 1917 Chaplin was also introducing more dramatic plot into his films, and mixing the comedy with sentiment.
      </p>
      <p>
       In Russia,
       <a href="/wiki/Yevgeni_Bauer" title="Yevgeni Bauer">
        Yevgeni Bauer
       </a>
       put a slow intensity of acting combined with Symbolist overtones onto film in a unique way.
      </p>
      <p>
       In Sweden,
       <a href="/wiki/Victor_Sj%C3%B6str%C3%B6m" title="Victor Sjöström">
        Victor Sjöström
       </a>
       made a series of films that combined the realities of people's lives with their surroundings in a striking manner, while Mauritz Stiller developed sophisticated comedy to a new level.
      </p>
      <p>
       In Germany,
       <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Lubitsch" title="Ernst Lubitsch">
        Ernst Lubitsch
       </a>
       got his inspiration from the stage work of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Max_Reinhardt_(director)" title="Max Reinhardt (director)">
        Max Reinhardt
       </a>
       , both in bourgeois comedy and in spectacle, and applied this to his films, culminating in his
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Doll_(1919_film)" title="The Doll (1919 film)">
         die Puppe
        </a>
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        The Doll
       </i>
       ),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Oyster_Princess" title="The Oyster Princess">
         die Austernprinzessin
        </a>
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        The Oyster Princess
       </i>
       ) and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Madame_DuBarry_(1919_film)" title="Madame DuBarry (1919 film)">
         Madame DuBarry
        </a>
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Hollywood_triumphant">
        Hollywood triumphant
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Hollywood triumphant">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charlie_Chaplin_with_doll.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="609" data-file-width="435" height="350" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Charlie_Chaplin_with_doll.jpg/250px-Charlie_Chaplin_with_doll.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Charlie_Chaplin_with_doll.jpg/375px-Charlie_Chaplin_with_doll.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e7/Charlie_Chaplin_with_doll.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charlie_Chaplin_with_doll.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Chaplin" title="Charlie Chaplin">
          Charlie Chaplin
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       At the start of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_World_War" title="First World War">
        First World War
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_France" title="Cinema of France">
        French
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Italy" title="Cinema of Italy">
        Italian
       </a>
       cinema had been the most globally popular. The war came as a devastating interruption to European film industries. The American industry, or "Hollywood", as it was becoming known after
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hollywood,_Los_Angeles" title="Hollywood, Los Angeles">
        its new geographical center in California
       </a>
       , gained the position it has held, more or less, ever since: film factory for the world and exporting its product to most countries on earth.
      </p>
      <p>
       By the 1920s, the United States reached what is still its era of greatest-ever output, producing an average of 800
       <i>
        feature
       </i>
       films annually,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-filmsite.org_1-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-filmsite.org-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       or 82% of the global total (Eyman, 1997). The comedies of
       <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Chaplin" title="Charlie Chaplin">
        Charlie Chaplin
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Buster_Keaton" title="Buster Keaton">
        Buster Keaton
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Swashbuckler" title="Swashbuckler">
        swashbuckling
       </a>
       adventures of
       <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Fairbanks" title="Douglas Fairbanks">
        Douglas Fairbanks
       </a>
       and the romances of
       <a href="/wiki/Clara_Bow" title="Clara Bow">
        Clara Bow
       </a>
       , to cite just a few examples, made these performers' faces well-known on every continent. The Western visual norm that would become classical
       <a href="/wiki/Continuity_editing" title="Continuity editing">
        continuity editing
       </a>
       was developed and exported – although its adoption was slower in some non-Western countries without strong
       <a href="/wiki/Realism_(arts)" title="Realism (arts)">
        realist
       </a>
       traditions in art and drama, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Japan" title="Cinema of Japan">
        Japan
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       This development was contemporary with the growth of the
       <a href="/wiki/Studio_system" title="Studio system">
        studio system
       </a>
       and its greatest publicity method, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Star_system_(film)" title="Star system (film)">
        star system
       </a>
       , which characterized American film for decades to come and provided models for other film industries. The studios' efficient, top-down control over all stages of their product enabled a new and ever-growing level of lavish production and technical sophistication. At the same time, the system's commercial regimentation and focus on glamorous
       <a href="/wiki/Escapism" title="Escapism">
        escapism
       </a>
       discouraged daring and ambition beyond a certain degree, a prime example being the brief but still legendary directing career of the iconoclastic
       <a href="/wiki/Erich_von_Stroheim" title="Erich von Stroheim">
        Erich von Stroheim
       </a>
       in the late teens and the '20s.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sound_era">
        Sound era
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Sound era">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Sound_film" title="Sound film">
        Sound film
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/1930s_in_film" title="1930s in film">
        1930s in film
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DonJuanPoster2.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="677" height="355" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/DonJuanPoster2.jpg/250px-DonJuanPoster2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/DonJuanPoster2.jpg/375px-DonJuanPoster2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/DonJuanPoster2.jpg/500px-DonJuanPoster2.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DonJuanPoster2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Don_Juan_(1926_film)" title="Don Juan (1926 film)">
          Don Juan
         </a>
         is the first feature-length film to utilize the Vitaphone sound-on-disc sound system with a synchronized musical score and sound effects, though it has no spoken dialogue.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       During late 1927, Warners released
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Jazz_Singer" title="The Jazz Singer">
         The Jazz Singer
        </a>
       </i>
       , which was mostly silent but contained what is generally regarded as the first synchronized dialogue (and singing) in a feature film; but this process was actually accomplished first by
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_Taze_Russell" title="Charles Taze Russell">
        Charles Taze Russell
       </a>
       in 1914 with the lengthy film
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Photo-Drama_of_Creation" title="The Photo-Drama of Creation">
         The Photo-Drama of Creation
        </a>
       </i>
       . This drama consisted of picture slides and moving pictures synchronized with phonograph records of talks and music. The early
       <a href="/wiki/Sound-on-disc" title="Sound-on-disc">
        sound-on-disc
       </a>
       processes such as Vitaphone were soon superseded by
       <a href="/wiki/Sound-on-film" title="Sound-on-film">
        sound-on-film
       </a>
       methods like Fox
       <a href="/wiki/Movietone_sound_system" title="Movietone sound system">
        Movietone
       </a>
       , DeForest
       <a href="/wiki/Phonofilm" title="Phonofilm">
        Phonofilm
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/RCA_Photophone" title="RCA Photophone">
        RCA Photophone
       </a>
       . The trend convinced the largely reluctant industrialists that "talking pictures", or "talkies", were the future. A lot of attempts were made before the success of
       <i>
        The Jazz Singer
       </i>
       , that can be seen in the
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_film_sound_systems" title="List of film sound systems">
        List of film sound systems
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The change was remarkably swift. By the end of 1929, Hollywood was almost all-talkie, with several competing sound systems (soon to be standardized). Total changeover was slightly slower in the rest of the world, principally for economic reasons. Cultural reasons were also a factor in countries like
       <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_China" title="Cinema of China">
        China
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Japan" title="Cinema of Japan">
        Japan
       </a>
       , where silents co-existed successfully with sound well into the 1930s, indeed producing what would be some of the most revered classics in those countries, like
       <a href="/wiki/Wu_Yonggang" title="Wu Yonggang">
        Wu Yonggang
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Goddess_(1934_film)" title="The Goddess (1934 film)">
         The Goddess
        </a>
       </i>
       (China, 1934) and
       <a href="/wiki/Yasujir%C5%8D_Ozu" title="Yasujirō Ozu">
        Yasujirō Ozu
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        I Was Born, But...
       </i>
       (Japan, 1932). But even in Japan, a figure such as the
       <i>
        benshi
       </i>
       , the live narrator who was a major part of Japanese silent cinema, found his acting career was ending.
      </p>
      <p>
       Sound further tightened the grip of major studios in numerous countries: the vast expense of the transition overwhelmed smaller competitors, while the novelty of sound lured vastly larger audiences for those producers that remained. In the case of the U.S., some historians credit sound with saving the Hollywood studio system in the face of the
       <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">
        Great Depression
       </a>
       (Parkinson, 1995). Thus began what is now often called "The Golden Age of Hollywood", which refers roughly to the period beginning with the introduction of sound until the late 1940s. The American cinema reached its peak of efficiently manufactured glamour and global appeal during this period. The top actors of the era are now thought of as the classic film stars, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Clark_Gable" title="Clark Gable">
        Clark Gable
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Katharine_Hepburn" title="Katharine Hepburn">
        Katharine Hepburn
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Humphrey_Bogart" title="Humphrey Bogart">
        Humphrey Bogart
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Greta_Garbo" title="Greta Garbo">
        Greta Garbo
       </a>
       , and the greatest box office draw of the 1930s, child performer
       <a href="/wiki/Shirley_Temple" title="Shirley Temple">
        Shirley Temple
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Creative_impact_of_sound">
        Creative impact of sound
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Creative impact of sound">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:104px;max-width:104px">
        <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:102px;max-width:102px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Clark_Gable_as_Rhett_Butler_in_Gone_With_the_Wind_trailer.jpg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="1208" data-file-width="1392" height="87" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Clark_Gable_as_Rhett_Butler_in_Gone_With_the_Wind_trailer.jpg/100px-Clark_Gable_as_Rhett_Butler_in_Gone_With_the_Wind_trailer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Clark_Gable_as_Rhett_Butler_in_Gone_With_the_Wind_trailer.jpg/150px-Clark_Gable_as_Rhett_Butler_in_Gone_With_the_Wind_trailer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Clark_Gable_as_Rhett_Butler_in_Gone_With_the_Wind_trailer.jpg/200px-Clark_Gable_as_Rhett_Butler_in_Gone_With_the_Wind_trailer.jpg 2x" width="100"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          Clark Gable
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:102px;max-width:102px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vivien_Leigh_as_Scarlett_OHara_in_Gone_With_the_Wind_trailer.jpg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="1324" data-file-width="1764" height="75" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Vivien_Leigh_as_Scarlett_OHara_in_Gone_With_the_Wind_trailer.jpg/100px-Vivien_Leigh_as_Scarlett_OHara_in_Gone_With_the_Wind_trailer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Vivien_Leigh_as_Scarlett_OHara_in_Gone_With_the_Wind_trailer.jpg/150px-Vivien_Leigh_as_Scarlett_OHara_in_Gone_With_the_Wind_trailer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Vivien_Leigh_as_Scarlett_OHara_in_Gone_With_the_Wind_trailer.jpg/200px-Vivien_Leigh_as_Scarlett_OHara_in_Gone_With_the_Wind_trailer.jpg 2x" width="100"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          Vivien Leigh
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:102px;max-width:102px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leslie_Howard_as_Ashley_Wilkes_in_Gone_With_the_Wind_trailer.jpg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="1324" data-file-width="1720" height="77" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Leslie_Howard_as_Ashley_Wilkes_in_Gone_With_the_Wind_trailer.jpg/100px-Leslie_Howard_as_Ashley_Wilkes_in_Gone_With_the_Wind_trailer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Leslie_Howard_as_Ashley_Wilkes_in_Gone_With_the_Wind_trailer.jpg/150px-Leslie_Howard_as_Ashley_Wilkes_in_Gone_With_the_Wind_trailer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Leslie_Howard_as_Ashley_Wilkes_in_Gone_With_the_Wind_trailer.jpg/200px-Leslie_Howard_as_Ashley_Wilkes_in_Gone_With_the_Wind_trailer.jpg 2x" width="100"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          Leslie Howard
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:102px;max-width:102px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Olivia_de_Havilland_as_Melanie_Hamilton_in_Gone_With_the_Wind_trailer.jpg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="1360" data-file-width="1816" height="75" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Olivia_de_Havilland_as_Melanie_Hamilton_in_Gone_With_the_Wind_trailer.jpg/100px-Olivia_de_Havilland_as_Melanie_Hamilton_in_Gone_With_the_Wind_trailer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Olivia_de_Havilland_as_Melanie_Hamilton_in_Gone_With_the_Wind_trailer.jpg/150px-Olivia_de_Havilland_as_Melanie_Hamilton_in_Gone_With_the_Wind_trailer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Olivia_de_Havilland_as_Melanie_Hamilton_in_Gone_With_the_Wind_trailer.jpg/200px-Olivia_de_Havilland_as_Melanie_Hamilton_in_Gone_With_the_Wind_trailer.jpg 2x" width="100"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          Olivia de Havilland
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Creatively, however, the rapid transition was a difficult one, and in some ways, film briefly reverted to the conditions of its earliest days. The late '20s were full of static, stagey talkies as artists in front of and behind the camera struggled with the stringent limitations of the early sound equipment and their own uncertainty as to how to utilize the new medium. Many stage performers, directors and writers were introduced to cinema as producers sought personnel experienced in dialogue-based storytelling. Many major silent filmmakers and actors were unable to adjust and found their careers severely curtailed or even ended.
      </p>
      <p>
       This awkward period was fairly short-lived. 1929 was a watershed year:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Wellman" title="William Wellman">
        William Wellman
       </a>
       with
       <i>
        Chinatown Nights
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        The Man I Love
       </i>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Rouben_Mamoulian" title="Rouben Mamoulian">
        Rouben Mamoulian
       </a>
       with
       <i>
        Applause
       </i>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Hitchcock" title="Alfred Hitchcock">
        Alfred Hitchcock
       </a>
       with
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Blackmail_(1929_film)" title="Blackmail (1929 film)">
         Blackmail
        </a>
       </i>
       (Britain's first sound feature), were among the directors to bring greater fluidity to talkies and experiment with the expressive use of sound (Eyman, 1997). In this, they both benefited from, and pushed further, technical advances in microphones and cameras, and capabilities for editing and post-synchronizing sound (rather than recording all sound directly at the time of filming).
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Walt_Disney_Snow_white_1937_trailer_screenshot_(13).jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="388" data-file-width="500" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Walt_Disney_Snow_white_1937_trailer_screenshot_%2813%29.jpg/250px-Walt_Disney_Snow_white_1937_trailer_screenshot_%2813%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Walt_Disney_Snow_white_1937_trailer_screenshot_%2813%29.jpg/375px-Walt_Disney_Snow_white_1937_trailer_screenshot_%2813%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cd/Walt_Disney_Snow_white_1937_trailer_screenshot_%2813%29.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Walt_Disney_Snow_white_1937_trailer_screenshot_(13).jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney" title="Walt Disney">
          Walt Disney
         </a>
         introduces each of the seven dwarfs in a scene from the original 1937 Snow White theatrical trailer.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Sound films emphasized black history and benefited different
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genres" title="Genres">
        genres
       </a>
       more so than silents did. Most obviously, the
       <a href="/wiki/Musical_film" title="Musical film">
        musical film
       </a>
       was born; the first classic-style Hollywood musical was
       <i>
        The Broadway Melody
       </i>
       (1929) and the form would find its first major creator in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Choreographer" title="Choreographer">
        choreographer
       </a>
       /director
       <a href="/wiki/Busby_Berkeley" title="Busby Berkeley">
        Busby Berkeley
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/42nd_Street_(film)" title="42nd Street (film)">
         42nd Street
        </a>
       </i>
       , 1933,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Dames" title="Dames">
         Dames
        </a>
       </i>
       , 1934). In France, avant-garde director
       <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Clair" title="René Clair">
        René Clair
       </a>
       made
       <a href="/wiki/Surrealism" title="Surrealism">
        surreal
       </a>
       use of song and dance in comedies like
       <i>
        Under the Roofs of Paris
       </i>
       (1930) and
       <i>
        Le Million
       </i>
       (1931).
       <a href="/wiki/Universal_Pictures" title="Universal Pictures">
        Universal Pictures
       </a>
       begin releasing
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gothic_horror" title="Gothic horror">
        gothic horror
       </a>
       films like
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Dracula_(1931_English-language_film)" title="Dracula (1931 English-language film)">
         Dracula
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frankenstein_(1931)" title="Frankenstein (1931)">
         Frankenstein
        </a>
       </i>
       (both 1931). In 1933, RKO released
       <a href="/wiki/Merian_C._Cooper" title="Merian C. Cooper">
        Merian C. Cooper
       </a>
       's classic "giant monster" film
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_Kong_(1933)" title="King Kong (1933)">
         King Kong
        </a>
       </i>
       . The trend thrived best in
       <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_India" title="Cinema of India">
        India
       </a>
       , where the influence of the country's traditional song-and-dance drama made the musical the basic form of most sound films (Cook, 1990); virtually unnoticed by the Western world for decades, this Indian popular cinema would nevertheless become the world's most prolific. (
       <i>
        See also
        <a href="/wiki/Bollywood" title="Bollywood">
         Bollywood
        </a>
        .
       </i>
       )
      </p>
      <p>
       At this time, American
       <a href="/wiki/Gangster_film" title="Gangster film">
        gangster films
       </a>
       like
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Little_Caesar_(film)" title="Little Caesar (film)">
         Little Caesar
        </a>
       </i>
       and Wellman's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Public_Enemy" title="The Public Enemy">
         The Public Enemy
        </a>
       </i>
       (both 1931) became popular. Dialogue now took precedence over "slapstick" in Hollywood comedies: the fast-paced, witty banter of
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Front_Page" title="The Front Page">
         The Front Page
        </a>
       </i>
       (1931) or
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/It_Happened_One_Night" title="It Happened One Night">
         It Happened One Night
        </a>
       </i>
       (1934), the sexual double entrendres of
       <a href="/wiki/Mae_West" title="Mae West">
        Mae West
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/She_Done_Him_Wrong" title="She Done Him Wrong">
         She Done Him Wrong
        </a>
       </i>
       , 1933) or the often subversively anarchic nonsense talk of the
       <a href="/wiki/Marx_Brothers" title="Marx Brothers">
        Marx Brothers
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Duck_Soup_(1933_film)" title="Duck Soup (1933 film)">
         Duck Soup
        </a>
       </i>
       , 1933).
       <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney" title="Walt Disney">
        Walt Disney
       </a>
       , who had previously been in the short cartoon business, stepped into feature films with the first English-speaking animated feature
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Snow_White_and_the_Seven_Dwarfs_(1937_film)" title="Snow White and the Seven Dwarfs (1937 film)">
         Snow White and the Seven Dwarfs
        </a>
       </i>
       ; released by
       <a href="/wiki/RKO_Pictures" title="RKO Pictures">
        RKO Pictures
       </a>
       in 1937. 1939, a major year for American cinema, brought such films as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Wizard_of_Oz_(1939_film)" title="The Wizard of Oz (1939 film)">
         The Wizard of Oz
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Gone_with_the_Wind_(film)" title="Gone with the Wind (film)">
         Gone with The Wind
        </a>
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Color_in_cinema">
        Color in cinema
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Color in cinema">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Previously, it was believed that color films were first projected in 1909 at the
       <a href="/wiki/Palace_Theatre,_London" title="Palace Theatre, London">
        Palace Theatre
       </a>
       in London (the main problem with the color being that the technique, created by George Smith, (
       <a href="/wiki/Kinemacolor" title="Kinemacolor">
        Kinemacolor
       </a>
       ) only used two colors: green and red, which were mixed additively). But in fact, it was in 1901 when the first color film in history was created. This untitled film was directed by photographer
       <a href="/wiki/Edward_Raymond_Turner" title="Edward Raymond Turner">
        Edward Raymond Turner
       </a>
       and his patron
       <a href="/wiki/Fred_Lee_(cricketer,_born_1871)" title="Fred Lee (cricketer, born 1871)">
        Frederick Marshall Lee
       </a>
       . The way they did it was to use black and white film rolls, but have green, red, and blue filters go over the camera individually as it shot. To complete the film, they joined the original footage and filters on a special projector. However, both the shooting of the film and its projection suffered from major unrelated issues that, eventually, sank the idea.
      </p>
      <p>
       Subsequently, in 1916, the
       <a href="/wiki/Technicolor" title="Technicolor">
        technicolor
       </a>
       technique arrived (trichromatic procedure (green, red, blue). Its use required a triple photographic impression, incorporation of chromatic filters and cameras of enormous dimensions). The first audiovisual piece that was completely realized with this technique was the short of
       <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney" title="Walt Disney">
        Walt Disney
       </a>
       "
       <a href="/wiki/Flowers_and_Trees" title="Flowers and Trees">
        Flowers and Trees
       </a>
       ", directed by
       <a href="/wiki/Burt_Gillett" title="Burt Gillett">
        Burt Gillett
       </a>
       in 1932. Even so, the first film to be performed with this technique will be "The Vanities Fair" (1935) by Rouben Mamoulian. Later on, the technicolor was extended mainly in the musical field as "
       <a href="/wiki/The_Wizard_of_Oz_(1939_film)" title="The Wizard of Oz (1939 film)">
        The Wizard of Oz
       </a>
       " or "
       <a href="/wiki/Singin%27_in_the_Rain" title="Singin' in the Rain">
        Singing in the rain
       </a>
       ", in films such as "
       <a href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Robin_Hood" title="The Adventures of Robin Hood">
        The Adventures of Robin Hood
       </a>
       " or the animation film, "
       <a href="/wiki/Snow_White_and_the_Seven_Dwarfs_(1937_film)" title="Snow White and the Seven Dwarfs (1937 film)">
        Snow White and the Seven Dwarfs
       </a>
       ".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esquire1_28-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-esquire1-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II_and_its_aftermath">
        World War II and its aftermath
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: World War II and its aftermath">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/1940s_in_film" title="1940s in film">
        1940s in film
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The desire for wartime propaganda against the opposition created a renaissance in the film industry in Britain, with realistic war dramas like
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/49th_Parallel_(film)" title="49th Parallel (film)">
         49th Parallel
        </a>
       </i>
       (1941),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Went_the_Day_Well%3F" title="Went the Day Well?">
         Went the Day Well?
        </a>
       </i>
       (1942),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Way_Ahead" title="The Way Ahead">
         The Way Ahead
        </a>
       </i>
       (1944) and
       <a href="/wiki/No%C3%ABl_Coward" title="Noël Coward">
        Noël Coward
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/David_Lean" title="David Lean">
        David Lean
       </a>
       's celebrated naval film
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/In_Which_We_Serve" title="In Which We Serve">
         In Which We Serve
        </a>
       </i>
       in 1942, which won a special
       <a href="/wiki/Academy_Awards" title="Academy Awards">
        Academy Award
       </a>
       . These existed alongside more flamboyant films like
       <a href="/wiki/Michael_Powell" title="Michael Powell">
        Michael Powell
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Emeric_Pressburger" title="Emeric Pressburger">
        Emeric Pressburger
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Life_and_Death_of_Colonel_Blimp" title="The Life and Death of Colonel Blimp">
         The Life and Death of Colonel Blimp
        </a>
       </i>
       (1943),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/A_Canterbury_Tale" title="A Canterbury Tale">
         A Canterbury Tale
        </a>
       </i>
       (1944) and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/A_Matter_of_Life_and_Death_(film)" title="A Matter of Life and Death (film)">
         A Matter of Life and Death
        </a>
       </i>
       (1946), as well as
       <a href="/wiki/Laurence_Olivier" title="Laurence Olivier">
        Laurence Olivier
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/1944_in_film" title="1944 in film">
        1944 film
       </a>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Henry_V_(1944_film)" title="Henry V (1944 film)">
         Henry V
        </a>
       </i>
       , based on the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shakespearean_histories" title="Shakespearean histories">
        Shakespearean history
       </a>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Henry_V_(play)" title="Henry V (play)">
         Henry V
        </a>
       </i>
       . The success of
       <i>
        Snow White and the Seven Dwarfs
       </i>
       allowed Disney to make more animated features like
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Pinocchio_(1940_film)" title="Pinocchio (1940 film)">
         Pinocchio
        </a>
       </i>
       (1940),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Fantasia_(1940_film)" title="Fantasia (1940 film)">
         Fantasia
        </a>
       </i>
       (1940),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Dumbo" title="Dumbo">
         Dumbo
        </a>
       </i>
       (1941) and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Bambi" title="Bambi">
         Bambi
        </a>
       </i>
       (1942).
      </p>
      <p>
       The onset of US involvement in World War II also brought a proliferation of films as both
       <a href="/wiki/Patriotism" title="Patriotism">
        patriotism
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">
        propaganda
       </a>
       . American propaganda films included
       <i>
        Desperate Journey
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Mrs._Miniver" title="Mrs. Miniver">
         Mrs. Miniver
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Forever and a Day
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Objective Burma
       </i>
       . Notable American films from the war years include the anti-Nazi
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Watch_on_the_Rhine" title="Watch on the Rhine">
         Watch on the Rhine
        </a>
       </i>
       (1943), scripted by
       <a href="/wiki/Dashiell_Hammett" title="Dashiell Hammett">
        Dashiell Hammett
       </a>
       ;
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Shadow_of_a_Doubt" title="Shadow of a Doubt">
         Shadow of a Doubt
        </a>
       </i>
       (1943), Hitchcock's direction of a script by
       <a href="/wiki/Thornton_Wilder" title="Thornton Wilder">
        Thornton Wilder
       </a>
       ; the
       <a href="/wiki/George_M._Cohan" title="George M. Cohan">
        George M. Cohan
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biopic" title="Biopic">
        biopic
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Yankee_Doodle_Dandy" title="Yankee Doodle Dandy">
         Yankee Doodle Dandy
        </a>
       </i>
       (1942), starring
       <a href="/wiki/James_Cagney" title="James Cagney">
        James Cagney
       </a>
       , and the immensely popular
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Casablanca_(film)" title="Casablanca (film)">
         Casablanca
        </a>
       </i>
       , with
       <a href="/wiki/Humphrey_Bogart" title="Humphrey Bogart">
        Humphrey Bogart
       </a>
       . Bogart would star in 36 films between 1934 and 1942 including
       <a href="/wiki/John_Huston" title="John Huston">
        John Huston
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Maltese_Falcon_(1941_film)" title="The Maltese Falcon (1941 film)">
         The Maltese Falcon
        </a>
       </i>
       (1941), one of the first films now considered a classic
