       <a href="/wiki/Film_noir" title="Film noir">
        film noir
       </a>
       . In 1941,
       <a href="/wiki/RKO_Pictures" title="RKO Pictures">
        RKO Pictures
       </a>
       released
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Citizen_Kane" title="Citizen Kane">
         Citizen Kane
        </a>
       </i>
       made by
       <a href="/wiki/Orson_Welles" title="Orson Welles">
        Orson Welles
       </a>
       . It is often considered the greatest film of all time. It would set the stage for the modern motion picture, as it revolutionized film story telling.
      </p>
      <p>
       The strictures of wartime also brought an interest in more fantastical subjects. These included Britain's
       <a href="/wiki/Gainsborough_Pictures" title="Gainsborough Pictures">
        Gainsborough
       </a>
       melodramas (including
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Man_in_Grey" title="The Man in Grey">
         The Man in Grey
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Wicked_Lady" title="The Wicked Lady">
         The Wicked Lady
        </a>
       </i>
       ), and films like
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Here_Comes_Mr._Jordan" title="Here Comes Mr. Jordan">
         Here Comes Mr. Jordan
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Heaven_Can_Wait_(1943_film)" title="Heaven Can Wait (1943 film)">
         Heaven Can Wait
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        I Married a Witch
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blithe_Spirit_(1945_film)" title="Blithe Spirit (1945 film)">
         Blithe Spirit
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Val_Lewton" title="Val Lewton">
        Val Lewton
       </a>
       also produced a series of atmospheric and influential small-budget
       <a href="/wiki/Horror_film" title="Horror film">
        horror
       </a>
       films, some of the more famous examples being
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Cat_People_(1942_film)" title="Cat People (1942 film)">
         Cat People
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_the_Dead_(film)" title="Isle of the Dead (film)">
         Isle of the Dead
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Body_Snatcher_(film)" title="The Body Snatcher (film)">
         The Body Snatcher
        </a>
       </i>
       . The decade probably also saw the so-called "women's pictures", such as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Now,_Voyager" title="Now, Voyager">
         Now, Voyager
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Random_Harvest_(film)" title="Random Harvest (film)">
         Random Harvest
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Mildred_Pierce_(film)" title="Mildred Pierce (film)">
         Mildred Pierce
        </a>
       </i>
       at the peak of their popularity.
      </p>
      <p>
       1946 saw RKO Radio releasing
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/It%27s_a_Wonderful_Life" title="It's a Wonderful Life">
         It's a Wonderful Life
        </a>
       </i>
       directed by
       <a href="/wiki/Frank_Capra" title="Frank Capra">
        Frank Capra
       </a>
       . Soldiers returning from the war would provide the inspiration for films like
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Best_Years_of_Our_Lives" title="The Best Years of Our Lives">
         The Best Years of Our Lives
        </a>
       </i>
       , and many of those in the film industry had served in some capacity during the war.
       <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Fuller" title="Samuel Fuller">
        Samuel Fuller
       </a>
       's experiences in World War II would influence his largely autobiographical films of later decades such as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Big_Red_One" title="The Big Red One">
         The Big Red One
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Actor%27s_Studio" title="The Actor's Studio">
        The Actor's Studio
       </a>
       was founded in October 1947 by
       <a href="/wiki/Elia_Kazan" title="Elia Kazan">
        Elia Kazan
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Lewis_(actor)" title="Robert Lewis (actor)">
        Robert Lewis
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Cheryl_Crawford" title="Cheryl Crawford">
        Cheryl Crawford
       </a>
       , and the same year
       <a href="/wiki/Oskar_Fischinger" title="Oskar Fischinger">
        Oskar Fischinger
       </a>
       filmed
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Motion_Painting_No._1" title="Motion Painting No. 1">
         Motion Painting No. 1
        </a>
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1943,
       <i>
        Ossessione
       </i>
       was screened in Italy, marking the beginning of
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_neorealism" title="Italian neorealism">
        Italian neorealism
       </a>
       . Major films of this type during the 1940s included
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_Thieves" title="Bicycle Thieves">
         Bicycle Thieves
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Rome,_Open_City" title="Rome, Open City">
         Rome, Open City
        </a>
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/La_Terra_Trema" title="La Terra Trema">
         La Terra Trema
        </a>
       </i>
       . In 1952
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umberto_D" title="Umberto D">
         Umberto D
        </a>
       </i>
       was released, usually considered the last film of this type.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the late 1940s, in Britain,
       <a href="/wiki/Ealing_Studios" title="Ealing Studios">
        Ealing Studios
       </a>
       embarked on their series of celebrated comedies, including
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Whisky_Galore!_(1949_film)" title="Whisky Galore! (1949 film)">
         Whisky Galore!
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Passport_to_Pimlico" title="Passport to Pimlico">
         Passport to Pimlico
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Kind_Hearts_and_Coronets" title="Kind Hearts and Coronets">
         Kind Hearts and Coronets
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Man_in_the_White_Suit" title="The Man in the White Suit">
         The Man in the White Suit
        </a>
       </i>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Carol_Reed" title="Carol Reed">
        Carol Reed
       </a>
       directed his influential thrillers
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Odd_Man_Out" title="Odd Man Out">
         Odd Man Out
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Fallen_Idol_(film)" title="The Fallen Idol (film)">
         The Fallen Idol
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Third_Man" title="The Third Man">
         The Third Man
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/David_Lean" title="David Lean">
        David Lean
       </a>
       was also rapidly becoming a force in world cinema with
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Brief_Encounter" title="Brief Encounter">
         Brief Encounter
        </a>
       </i>
       and his
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">
        Dickens
       </a>
       adaptations
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Great_Expectations_(1946_film)" title="Great Expectations (1946 film)">
         Great Expectations
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Twist_(1948_film)" title="Oliver Twist (1948 film)">
         Oliver Twist
        </a>
       </i>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Michael_Powell" title="Michael Powell">
        Michael Powell
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Emeric_Pressburger" title="Emeric Pressburger">
        Emeric Pressburger
       </a>
       would experience the best of their creative partnership with films like
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Black_Narcissus" title="Black Narcissus">
         Black Narcissus
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Red_Shoes_(1948_film)" title="The Red Shoes (1948 film)">
         The Red Shoes
        </a>
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="1950s">
        1950s
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: 1950s">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/1950s_in_film" title="1950s in film">
        1950s in film
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charlton_Heston_as_Antony_in_Julius_Caesar,_B%26W_image_by_Chalmers_Butterfield.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="2840" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Charlton_Heston_as_Antony_in_Julius_Caesar%2C_B%26W_image_by_Chalmers_Butterfield.jpg/300px-Charlton_Heston_as_Antony_in_Julius_Caesar%2C_B%26W_image_by_Chalmers_Butterfield.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Charlton_Heston_as_Antony_in_Julius_Caesar%2C_B%26W_image_by_Chalmers_Butterfield.jpg/450px-Charlton_Heston_as_Antony_in_Julius_Caesar%2C_B%26W_image_by_Chalmers_Butterfield.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Charlton_Heston_as_Antony_in_Julius_Caesar%2C_B%26W_image_by_Chalmers_Butterfield.jpg/600px-Charlton_Heston_as_Antony_in_Julius_Caesar%2C_B%26W_image_by_Chalmers_Butterfield.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charlton_Heston_as_Antony_in_Julius_Caesar,_B%26W_image_by_Chalmers_Butterfield.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A production scene from the 1950 Hollywood film
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar_(1950_film)" title="Julius Caesar (1950 film)">
           Julius Caesar
          </a>
         </i>
         starring
         <a href="/wiki/Charlton_Heston" title="Charlton Heston">
          Charlton Heston
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/House_Un-American_Activities_Committee" title="House Un-American Activities Committee">
        House Un-American Activities Committee
       </a>
       investigated Hollywood in the early 1950s. Protested by the
       <a href="/wiki/Hollywood_blacklist" title="Hollywood blacklist">
        Hollywood Ten
       </a>
       before the committee, the hearings resulted in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blacklist" title="Blacklist">
        blacklisting
       </a>
       of many actors, writers and directors, including Chayefsky,
       <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Chaplin" title="Charlie Chaplin">
        Charlie Chaplin
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Dalton_Trumbo" title="Dalton Trumbo">
        Dalton Trumbo
       </a>
       , and many of these fled to Europe, especially the United Kingdom.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">
        Cold War era
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zeitgeist" title="Zeitgeist">
        zeitgeist
       </a>
       translated into a type of near-
       <a href="/wiki/Paranoia" title="Paranoia">
        paranoia
       </a>
       manifested in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theme_(literature)" title="Theme (literature)">
        themes
       </a>
       such as
       <a href="/wiki/Alien_invasion" title="Alien invasion">
        invading armies of evil aliens
       </a>
       , (
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_the_Body_Snatchers" title="Invasion of the Body Snatchers">
         Invasion of the Body Snatchers
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_War_of_the_Worlds_(1953_film)" title="The War of the Worlds (1953 film)">
         The War of the Worlds
        </a>
       </i>
       ); and
       <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">
        communist
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Fifth_column" title="Fifth column">
        fifth columnists
       </a>
       , (
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Manchurian_Candidate_(1962_film)" title="The Manchurian Candidate (1962 film)">
         The Manchurian Candidate
        </a>
       </i>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       During the immediate post-war years the cinematic industry was also threatened by television, and the increasing popularity of the medium meant that some film theatres would bankrupt and close. The demise of the "studio system" spurred the
       <a href="/wiki/Criticism" title="Criticism">
        self-commentary
       </a>
       of films like
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunset_Boulevard_(1950_film)" title="Sunset Boulevard (1950 film)">
         Sunset Boulevard
        </a>
       </i>
       (1950) and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Bad_and_the_Beautiful" title="The Bad and the Beautiful">
         The Bad and the Beautiful
        </a>
       </i>
       (1952).
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1950, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lettrists" title="Lettrists">
        Lettrists
       </a>
       avante-gardists caused riots at the
       <a href="/wiki/Cannes_Film_Festival" title="Cannes Film Festival">
        Cannes Film Festival
       </a>
       , when
       <a href="/wiki/Isidore_Isou" title="Isidore Isou">
        Isidore Isou
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        Treatise on Slime and Eternity
       </i>
       was screened. After their criticism of
       <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Chaplin" title="Charlie Chaplin">
        Charlie Chaplin
       </a>
       and split with the movement, the
       <a href="/wiki/Ultra-Lettrist" title="Ultra-Lettrist">
        Ultra-Lettrists
       </a>
       continued to cause disruptions when they showed their new
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypergraphics" title="Hypergraphics">
        hypergraphical
       </a>
       techniques. The most notorious film is
       <a href="/wiki/Guy_Debord" title="Guy Debord">
        Guy Debord
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        Howls for Sade
       </i>
       of 1952.
      </p>
      <p>
       Distressed by the increasing number of closed theatres, studios and companies would find new and innovative ways to bring audiences back. These included attempts to widen their appeal with new screen formats.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cinemascope" title="Cinemascope">
        Cinemascope
       </a>
       , which would remain a
       <a href="/wiki/20th_Century_Fox" title="20th Century Fox">
        20th Century Fox
       </a>
       distinction until 1967, was announced with 1953's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Robe_(film)" title="The Robe (film)">
         The Robe
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/VistaVision" title="VistaVision">
        VistaVision
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Cinerama" title="Cinerama">
        Cinerama
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Todd-AO" title="Todd-AO">
        Todd-AO
       </a>
       boasted a "bigger is better" approach to
       <a href="/wiki/Marketing" title="Marketing">
        marketing
       </a>
       films to a dwindling US audience. This resulted in the revival of epic films to take advantage of the new big screen formats. Some of the most successful examples of these
       <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">
        Biblical
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">
        historical
       </a>
       spectaculars include
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Ten_Commandments_(1956_film)" title="The Ten Commandments (1956 film)">
         The Ten Commandments
        </a>
       </i>
       (1956),
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Vikings_(film)" title="The Vikings (film)">
         The Vikings
        </a>
       </i>
       (1958),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Ben-Hur_(1959_film)" title="Ben-Hur (1959 film)">
         Ben-Hur
        </a>
       </i>
       (1959),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Spartacus_(film)" title="Spartacus (film)">
         Spartacus
        </a>
       </i>
       (1960) and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/El_Cid_(film)" title="El Cid (film)">
         El Cid
        </a>
       </i>
       (1961). Also during this period a number of other significant films were produced in
       <a href="/wiki/Todd-AO" title="Todd-AO">
        Todd-AO
       </a>
       , developed by
       <a href="/wiki/Mike_Todd" title="Mike Todd">
        Mike Todd
       </a>
       shortly before his death, including
       <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma!" title="Oklahoma!">
        Oklahoma!
       </a>
       (1955),
       <a href="/wiki/Around_the_World_in_80_Days_(1956_film)" title="Around the World in 80 Days (1956 film)">
        Around the World in 80 Days
       </a>
       (1956),
       <a href="/wiki/South_Pacific_(musical)" title="South Pacific (musical)">
        South Pacific
       </a>
       (1958) and
       <a href="/wiki/Cleopatra" title="Cleopatra">
        Cleopatra
       </a>
       (1963) plus many more.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Gimmick" title="Gimmick">
        Gimmicks
       </a>
       also proliferated to lure in audiences. The fad for
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/3-D_film" title="3-D film">
        3-D film
       </a>
       would last for only two years, 1952–1954, and helped sell
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/House_of_Wax_(1953_film)" title="House of Wax (1953 film)">
         House of Wax
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Creature_from_the_Black_Lagoon" title="Creature from the Black Lagoon">
         Creature from the Black Lagoon
        </a>
       </i>
       . Producer
       <a href="/wiki/William_Castle" title="William Castle">
        William Castle
       </a>
       would tout films featuring "Emergo" "Percepto", the first of a series of gimmicks that would remain popular marketing tools for Castle and others throughout the 1960s.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the U.S., a post-WW2 tendency toward questioning the establishment and societal norms and the early
       <a href="/wiki/Activism" title="Activism">
        activism
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Civil_rights_movement" title="Civil rights movement">
        civil rights movement
       </a>
       was reflected in Hollywood films such as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Blackboard_Jungle" title="Blackboard Jungle">
         Blackboard Jungle
        </a>
       </i>
       (1955),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/On_the_Waterfront" title="On the Waterfront">
         On the Waterfront
        </a>
       </i>
       (1954),
       <a href="/wiki/Paddy_Chayefsky" title="Paddy Chayefsky">
        Paddy Chayefsky
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Marty_(film)" title="Marty (film)">
         Marty
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Reginald_Rose" title="Reginald Rose">
        Reginald Rose
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/12_Angry_Men_(1957_film)" title="12 Angry Men (1957 film)">
         12 Angry Men
        </a>
       </i>
       (1957). Disney continued making animated films, notably;
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Cinderella_(1950_film)" title="Cinderella (1950 film)">
         Cinderella
        </a>
       </i>
       (1950),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Peter_Pan_(1953_film)" title="Peter Pan (1953 film)">
         Peter Pan
        </a>
       </i>
       (1953),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Lady_and_the_Tramp" title="Lady and the Tramp">
         Lady and the Tramp
        </a>
       </i>
       (1955), and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Sleeping_Beauty_(1959_film)" title="Sleeping Beauty (1959 film)">
         Sleeping Beauty
        </a>
       </i>
       (1959). He began, however, getting more involved in live action films, producing classics like
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/20,000_Leagues_Under_the_Sea_(1954_film)" title="20,000 Leagues Under the Sea (1954 film)">
         20,000 Leagues Under the Sea
        </a>
       </i>
       (1954), and
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Yeller_(1957_film)" title="Old Yeller (1957 film)">
         Old Yeller
        </a>
       </i>
       (1957). Television began competing seriously with films projected in theatres, but surprisingly it promoted more filmgoing rather than curtailing it.
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Limelight_(1952_film)" title="Limelight (1952 film)">
         Limelight
        </a>
       </i>
       is probably a unique film in at least one interesting respect. Its two leads,
       <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Chaplin" title="Charlie Chaplin">
        Charlie Chaplin
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Claire_Bloom" title="Claire Bloom">
        Claire Bloom
       </a>
       , were in the industry in no less than three different centuries. In the 19th Century, Chaplin made his theatrical debut at the age of eight, in 1897, in a clog dancing troupe, The Eight Lancaster Lads. In the 21st Century, Bloom is still enjoying a full and productive career, having appeared in dozens of films and television series produced up to and including 2013. She received particular acclaim for her role in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_King%27s_Speech" title="The King's Speech">
         The King's Speech
        </a>
       </i>
       (2010).
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Golden_age_of_Asian_cinema">
        Golden age of Asian cinema
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Golden age of Asian cinema">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asian_cinema" title="Asian cinema">
        Asian cinema
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Satyajit_Ray.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1040" data-file-width="800" height="325" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Satyajit_Ray.jpg/250px-Satyajit_Ray.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Satyajit_Ray.jpg/375px-Satyajit_Ray.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Satyajit_Ray.jpg/500px-Satyajit_Ray.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Satyajit_Ray.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Satyajit_Ray" title="Satyajit Ray">
          Satyajit Ray
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">
          Indian
         </a>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bengali_people" title="Bengali people">
          Bengali
         </a>
         film director.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Following the end of World War II in the 1940s, the following decade, the 1950s, marked a 'golden age' for non-English
       <a href="/wiki/World_cinema" title="World cinema">
        world cinema
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daniels_41-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Daniels-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       especially for
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asian_cinema" title="Asian cinema">
        Asian cinema
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lee_42-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lee-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Totaro_43-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Totaro-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Many of the most critically acclaimed Asian films of all time were produced during this decade, including
       <a href="/wiki/Yasujir%C5%8D_Ozu" title="Yasujirō Ozu">
        Yasujirō Ozu
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Story" title="Tokyo Story">
         Tokyo Story
        </a>
       </i>
       (1953),
       <a href="/wiki/Satyajit_Ray" title="Satyajit Ray">
        Satyajit Ray
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Apu_Trilogy" title="The Apu Trilogy">
         The Apu Trilogy
        </a>
       </i>
       (1955–1959) and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Jalsaghar" title="Jalsaghar">
         Jalsaghar
        </a>
       </i>
       (1958),
       <a href="/wiki/Kenji_Mizoguchi" title="Kenji Mizoguchi">
        Kenji Mizoguchi
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Ugetsu" title="Ugetsu">
         Ugetsu
        </a>
       </i>
       (1954) and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Sansho_the_Bailiff" title="Sansho the Bailiff">
         Sansho the Bailiff
        </a>
       </i>
       (1954),
       <a href="/wiki/Raj_Kapoor" title="Raj Kapoor">
        Raj Kapoor
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Awaara" title="Awaara">
         Awaara
        </a>
       </i>
       (1951),
       <a href="/wiki/Mikio_Naruse" title="Mikio Naruse">
        Mikio Naruse
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Floating_Clouds" title="Floating Clouds">
         Floating Clouds
        </a>
       </i>
       (1955),
       <a href="/wiki/Guru_Dutt" title="Guru Dutt">
        Guru Dutt
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Pyaasa" title="Pyaasa">
         Pyaasa
        </a>
       </i>
       (1957) and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Kaagaz_Ke_Phool" title="Kaagaz Ke Phool">
         Kaagaz Ke Phool
        </a>
       </i>
       (1959), and the
       <a href="/wiki/Akira_Kurosawa" title="Akira Kurosawa">
        Akira Kurosawa
       </a>
       films
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rashomon_(film)" title="Rashomon (film)">
         Rashomon
        </a>
       </i>
       (1950),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Ikiru" title="Ikiru">
         Ikiru
        </a>
       </i>
       (1952),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Seven_Samurai" title="Seven Samurai">
         Seven Samurai
        </a>
       </i>
       (1954) and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Throne_of_Blood" title="Throne of Blood">
         Throne of Blood
        </a>
       </i>
       (1957).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lee_42-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lee-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Totaro_43-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Totaro-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       During
       <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Japan" title="Cinema of Japan">
        Japanese cinema
       </a>
       's 'Golden Age' of the 1950s, successful films included
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rashomon_(film)" title="Rashomon (film)">
         Rashomon
        </a>
       </i>
       (1950),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Seven_Samurai" title="Seven Samurai">
         Seven Samurai
        </a>
       </i>
       (1954) and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Hidden_Fortress" title="The Hidden Fortress">
         The Hidden Fortress
        </a>
       </i>
       (1958) by
       <a href="/wiki/Akira_Kurosawa" title="Akira Kurosawa">
        Akira Kurosawa
       </a>
       , as well as
       <a href="/wiki/Yasujir%C5%8D_Ozu" title="Yasujirō Ozu">
        Yasujirō Ozu
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Story" title="Tokyo Story">
         Tokyo Story
        </a>
       </i>
       (1953) and
       <a href="/wiki/Ishir%C5%8D_Honda" title="Ishirō Honda">
        Ishirō Honda
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Godzilla_(1954_film)" title="Godzilla (1954 film)">
         Godzilla
        </a>
       </i>
       (1954).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These films have had a profound influence on world cinema. In particular, Kurosawa's
       <i>
        Seven Samurai
       </i>
       has been remade several times as
       <a href="/wiki/Western_(genre)" title="Western (genre)">
        Western films
       </a>
       , such as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Magnificent_Seven" title="The Magnificent Seven">
         The Magnificent Seven
        </a>
       </i>
       (1960) and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Battle_Beyond_the_Stars" title="Battle Beyond the Stars">
         Battle Beyond the Stars
        </a>
       </i>
       (1980), and has also inspired several
       <a href="/wiki/Bollywood" title="Bollywood">
        Bollywood
       </a>
       films, such as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Sholay" title="Sholay">
         Sholay
        </a>
       </i>
       (1975) and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/China_Gate_(1998_film)" title="China Gate (1998 film)">
         China Gate
        </a>
       </i>
       (1998).
       <i>
        Rashomon
       </i>
       was also remade as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Outrage" title="The Outrage">
         The Outrage
        </a>
       </i>
       (1964), and inspired films with "
       <a href="/wiki/Rashomon_effect" title="Rashomon effect">
        Rashomon effect
       </a>
       " storytelling methods, such as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Andha_Naal" title="Andha Naal">
         Andha Naal
        </a>
       </i>
       (1954),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Usual_Suspects" title="The Usual Suspects">
         The Usual Suspects
        </a>
       </i>
       (1995) and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Hero_(2002_film)" title="Hero (2002 film)">
         Hero
        </a>
       </i>
       (2002).
       <i>
        The Hidden Fortress
       </i>
       was also the inspiration behind
       <a href="/wiki/George_Lucas" title="George Lucas">
        George Lucas
       </a>
       '
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Star_Wars_(film)" title="Star Wars (film)">
         Star Wars
        </a>
       </i>
       (1977). Other famous Japanese filmmakers from this period include
       <a href="/wiki/Kenji_Mizoguchi" title="Kenji Mizoguchi">
        Kenji Mizoguchi
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Mikio_Naruse" title="Mikio Naruse">
        Mikio Naruse
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hiroshi_Inagaki" title="Hiroshi Inagaki">
        Hiroshi Inagaki
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Nagisa_Oshima" title="Nagisa Oshima">
        Nagisa Oshima
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lee_42-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lee-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Japanese cinema later became one of the main inspirations behind the
       <a href="/wiki/New_Hollywood" title="New Hollywood">
        New Hollywood
       </a>
       movement of the 1960s to 1980s.
      </p>
      <p>
       During
       <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_India" title="Cinema of India">
        Indian cinema
       </a>
       's 'Golden Age' of the 1950s, it was producing 200 films annually, while
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parallel_Cinema" title="Parallel Cinema">
        Indian independent films
       </a>
       gained greater recognition through international
       <a href="/wiki/Film_festival" title="Film festival">
        film festivals
       </a>
       . One of the most famous was
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Apu_Trilogy" title="The Apu Trilogy">
         The Apu Trilogy
        </a>
       </i>
       (1955–1959) from critically acclaimed
       <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_West_Bengal" title="Cinema of West Bengal">
        Bengali film
       </a>
       director
       <a href="/wiki/Satyajit_Ray" title="Satyajit Ray">
        Satyajit Ray
       </a>
       , whose films had a profound influence on world cinema, with directors such as Akira Kurosawa,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Martin_Scorsese" title="Martin Scorsese">
        Martin Scorsese
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_Ivory_(director)" title="James Ivory (director)">
        James Ivory
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Abbas_Kiarostami" title="Abbas Kiarostami">
        Abbas Kiarostami
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Elia_Kazan" title="Elia Kazan">
        Elia Kazan
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Truffaut" title="François Truffaut">
        François Truffaut
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Steven_Spielberg" title="Steven Spielberg">
        Steven Spielberg
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unmaderay_50-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-unmaderay-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMDbRay_51-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IMDbRay-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCSCcurrents_52-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-UCSCcurrents-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Saura" title="Carlos Saura">
        Carlos Saura
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Jean-Luc_Godard" title="Jean-Luc Godard">
        Jean-Luc Godard
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Isao_Takahata" title="Isao Takahata">
        Isao Takahata
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Gregory_Nava" title="Gregory Nava">
        Gregory Nava
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ira_Sachs" title="Ira Sachs">
        Ira Sachs
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Wes_Anderson" title="Wes Anderson">
        Wes Anderson
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Statesman_56-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-The_Statesman-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Danny_Boyle" title="Danny Boyle">
        Danny Boyle
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jivani_57-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jivani-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       being influenced by his cinematic style. According to Michael Sragow of
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Atlantic" title="The Atlantic">
         The Atlantic Monthly
        </a>
       </i>
       , the "youthful
       <a href="/wiki/Coming_of_age" title="Coming of age">
        coming-of-age
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drama_film" title="Drama film">
        dramas
       </a>
       that have flooded art houses since the mid-fifties owe a tremendous debt to the Apu trilogy".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Subrata_Mitra" title="Subrata Mitra">
        Subrata Mitra
       </a>
       's cinematographic technique of
       <a href="/wiki/Reflector_(photography)#Bounce_lighting" title="Reflector (photography)">
        bounce lighting
       </a>
       also originates from
       <i>
        The Apu Trilogy
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cinematographers_59-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-cinematographers-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Other famous Indian filmmakers from this period include
       <a href="/wiki/Guru_Dutt" title="Guru Dutt">
        Guru Dutt
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lee_42-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lee-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Ritwik_Ghatak" title="Ritwik Ghatak">
        Ritwik Ghatak
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Totaro_43-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Totaro-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Mrinal_Sen" title="Mrinal Sen">
        Mrinal Sen
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Raj_Kapoor" title="Raj Kapoor">
        Raj Kapoor
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bimal_Roy" title="Bimal Roy">
        Bimal Roy
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/K._Asif" title="K. Asif">
        K. Asif
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Mehboob_Khan" title="Mehboob Khan">
        Mehboob Khan
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cinemacom_60-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cinemacom-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_South_Korea" title="Cinema of South Korea">
        cinema of South Korea
       </a>
       also experienced a 'Golden Age' in the 1950s, beginning with director Lee Kyu-hwan's tremendously successful remake of
       <i>
        Chunhyang-jon
       </i>
       (1955).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marshall_61-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Marshall-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       That year also saw the release of
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangsan_Province_(film)" title="Yangsan Province (film)">
         Yangsan Province
        </a>
       </i>
       by the renowned director,
       <a href="/wiki/Kim_Ki-young" title="Kim Ki-young">
        Kim Ki-young
       </a>
       , marking the beginning of his productive career. Both the quality and quantity of filmmaking had increased rapidly by the end of the 1950s. South Korean films, such as Lee Byeong-il's 1956 comedy
       <i>
        Sijibganeun nal (The Wedding Day)
       </i>
       , had begun winning international awards. In contrast to the beginning of the 1950s, when only 5 films were made per year, 111 films were produced in South Korea in 1959.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The 1950s was also a 'Golden Age' for
       <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_the_Philippines" title="Cinema of the Philippines">
        Philippine cinema
       </a>
       , with the emergence of more artistic and mature films, and significant improvement in cinematic techniques among filmmakers. The studio system produced frenetic activity in the local film industry as many films were made annually and several local talents started to earn recognition abroad. The premiere Philippine directors of the era included
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gerardo_de_Leon" title="Gerardo de Leon">
        Gerardo de Leon
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Gregorio_Fern%C3%A1ndez_(director)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Gregorio Fernández (director) (page does not exist)">
        Gregorio Fernández
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Romero" title="Eddie Romero">
        Eddie Romero
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lamberto_Avellana" title="Lamberto Avellana">
        Lamberto Avellana
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cirio_Santiago" title="Cirio Santiago">
        Cirio Santiago
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-curtain_63-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-curtain-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="1960s">
        1960s
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: 1960s">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/1960s_in_film" title="1960s in film">
        1960s in film
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       During the 1960s, the studio system in
       <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_the_United_States" title="Cinema of the United States">
        Hollywood
       </a>
       declined, because many films were now being made on location in other countries, or using studio facilities abroad, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Pinewood_Studios" title="Pinewood Studios">
        Pinewood
       </a>
       in the UK and
       <a href="/wiki/Cinecitt%C3%A0" title="Cinecittà">
        Cinecittà
       </a>
       in Rome. "Hollywood" films were still largely aimed at family audiences, and it was often the more old-fashioned films that produced the studios' biggest successes. Productions like
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Mary_Poppins_(film)" title="Mary Poppins (film)">
         Mary Poppins
        </a>
       </i>
       (1964),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/My_Fair_Lady_(film)" title="My Fair Lady (film)">
         My Fair Lady
        </a>
       </i>
       (1964) and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Sound_of_Music_(film)" title="The Sound of Music (film)">
         The Sound of Music
        </a>
       </i>
       (1965) were among the biggest money-makers of the decade. The growth in independent producers and production companies, and the increase in the power of individual actors also contributed to the decline of traditional Hollywood studio production.
      </p>
      <p>
       There was also an increasing awareness of foreign language cinema in America during this period. During the late 1950s and 1960s, the
       <a href="/wiki/French_New_Wave" title="French New Wave">
        French New Wave
       </a>
       directors such as
       <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Truffaut" title="François Truffaut">
        François Truffaut
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Jean-Luc_Godard" title="Jean-Luc Godard">
        Jean-Luc Godard
       </a>
       produced films such as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_400_Blows" title="The 400 Blows">
         Les quatre cents coups
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Breathless_(1960_film)" title="Breathless (1960 film)">
         Breathless
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jules_et_Jim" title="Jules et Jim">
         Jules et Jim
        </a>
       </i>
       which broke the rules of Hollywood cinema's
       <a href="/wiki/Narrative_structure" title="Narrative structure">
        narrative structure
       </a>
       . As well, audiences were becoming aware of Italian films like
       <a href="/wiki/Federico_Fellini" title="Federico Fellini">
        Federico Fellini
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/La_Dolce_Vita" title="La Dolce Vita">
         La Dolce Vita
        </a>
       </i>
       and the stark dramas of Sweden's
       <a href="/wiki/Ingmar_Bergman" title="Ingmar Bergman">
        Ingmar Bergman
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In Britain, the "Free Cinema" of
       <a href="/wiki/Lindsay_Anderson" title="Lindsay Anderson">
        Lindsay Anderson
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tony_Richardson" title="Tony Richardson">
        Tony Richardson
       </a>
       and others lead to a group of realistic and innovative dramas including
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Saturday_Night_and_Sunday_Morning_(film)" title="Saturday Night and Sunday Morning (film)">
         Saturday Night and Sunday Morning
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/A_Kind_of_Loving_(film)" title="A Kind of Loving (film)">
         A Kind of Loving
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/This_Sporting_Life" title="This Sporting Life">
         This Sporting Life
        </a>
       </i>
       . Other British films such as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Repulsion_(film)" title="Repulsion (film)">
         Repulsion
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Darling_(1965_film)" title="Darling (1965 film)">
         Darling
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Alfie_(1966_film)" title="Alfie (1966 film)">
         Alfie
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Blowup" title="Blowup">
         Blowup
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Georgy_Girl" title="Georgy Girl">
         Georgy Girl
        </a>
       </i>
       (all in 1965–1966) helped to reduce prohibitions sex and nudity on screen, while the casual sex and violence of the
       <a href="/wiki/James_Bond" title="James Bond">
        James Bond
       </a>
       films, beginning with
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Dr._No_(film)" title="Dr. No (film)">
         Dr. No
        </a>
       </i>
       in 1962 would render the series popular worldwide.
      </p>
      <p>
       During the 1960s,
       <a href="/wiki/Ousmane_Semb%C3%A8ne" title="Ousmane Sembène">
        Ousmane Sembène
       </a>
       produced several French- and
       <a href="/wiki/Wolof_language" title="Wolof language">
        Wolof-language
       </a>
       films and became the "father" of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_Cinema" title="African Cinema">
        African Cinema
       </a>
       . In Latin America, the dominance of the "Hollywood" model was challenged by many film makers.
       <a href="/wiki/Fernando_Solanas" title="Fernando Solanas">
        Fernando Solanas
       </a>
       and Octavio Gettino called for a politically engaged
       <a href="/wiki/Third_Cinema" title="Third Cinema">
        Third Cinema
       </a>
       in contrast to Hollywood and the European
       <a href="/wiki/Auteur" title="Auteur">
        auteur
       </a>
       cinema.
      </p>
      <p>
       Further, the nuclear paranoia of the age, and the threat of an apocalyptic nuclear exchange (like the 1962 close-call with the
       <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">
        USSR
       </a>
       during the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuban_missile_crisis" title="Cuban missile crisis">
        Cuban missile crisis
       </a>
       ) prompted a reaction within the film community as well. Films like
       <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Kubrick" title="Stanley Kubrick">
        Stanley Kubrick
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Dr._Strangelove" title="Dr. Strangelove">
         Dr. Strangelove
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fail-Safe_(1964_film)" title="Fail-Safe (1964 film)">
         Fail Safe
        </a>
       </i>
       with
       <a href="/wiki/Henry_Fonda" title="Henry Fonda">
        Henry Fonda
       </a>
       were produced in a Hollywood that was once known for its overt patriotism and wartime propaganda.
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Documentary_film" title="Documentary film">
        documentary film
       </a>
       the sixties saw the blossoming of
       <a href="/wiki/Direct_Cinema" title="Direct Cinema">
        Direct Cinema
       </a>
       , an observational style of film making as well as the advent of more overtly partisan films like
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/In_the_Year_of_the_Pig" title="In the Year of the Pig">
         In the Year of the Pig
        </a>
       </i>
       about the
       <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">
        Vietnam War
       </a>
       by
       <a href="/wiki/Emile_de_Antonio" title="Emile de Antonio">
        Emile de Antonio
       </a>
       . By the late 1960s however, Hollywood filmmakers were beginning to create more innovative and groundbreaking films that reflected the social revolution taken over much of the western world such as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Bonnie_and_Clyde_(film)" title="Bonnie and Clyde (film)">
         Bonnie and Clyde
        </a>
       </i>
       (1967),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Graduate" title="The Graduate">
         The Graduate
        </a>
       </i>
       (1967),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)" title="2001: A Space Odyssey (film)">
         2001: A Space Odyssey
        </a>
       </i>
       (1968),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Rosemary%27s_Baby_(film)" title="Rosemary's Baby (film)">
         Rosemary's Baby
        </a>
       </i>
       (1968),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Midnight_Cowboy" title="Midnight Cowboy">
         Midnight Cowboy
        </a>
       </i>
       (1969),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Easy_Rider" title="Easy Rider">
         Easy Rider
        </a>
       </i>
       (1969) and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Wild_Bunch" title="The Wild Bunch">
         The Wild Bunch
        </a>
       </i>
       (1969).
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Bonnie_and_Clyde_(film)" title="Bonnie and Clyde (film)">
         Bonnie and Clyde
        </a>
       </i>
       is often considered the beginning of the so-called
       <a href="/wiki/New_Hollywood" title="New Hollywood">
        New Hollywood
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In Japanese cinema, Academy Award winning director
       <a href="/wiki/Akira_Kurosawa" title="Akira Kurosawa">
        Akira Kurosawa
       </a>
       produced
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Yojimbo_(film)" title="Yojimbo (film)">
         Yojimbo
        </a>
       </i>
       (1961), which like his previous films also had a profound influence around the world. The influence of this film is most apparent in
       <a href="/wiki/Sergio_Leone" title="Sergio Leone">
        Sergio Leone
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/A_Fistful_of_Dollars" title="A Fistful of Dollars">
         A Fistful of Dollars
        </a>
       </i>
       (1964) and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Walter_Hill_(filmmaker)" title="Walter Hill (filmmaker)">
        Walter Hill
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Last_Man_Standing_(1996_film)" title="Last Man Standing (1996 film)">
         Last Man Standing
        </a>
       </i>
       (1996).
       <i>
        Yojimbo
       </i>
       was also the origin of the "
       <a href="/wiki/Man_with_No_Name" title="Man with No Name">
        Man with No Name
       </a>
       " trend.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="1970s">
        1970s
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: 1970s">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/1970s_in_film" title="1970s in film">
        1970s in film
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/New_Hollywood" title="New Hollywood">
        New Hollywood
       </a>
       was the period following the decline of the
       <a href="/wiki/Studio_system" title="Studio system">
        studio system
       </a>
       during the 1950s and 1960s and the end of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Production_code" title="Production code">
        production code
       </a>
       , (which was replaced in 1968 by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MPAA_film_rating_system" title="MPAA film rating system">
        MPAA film rating system
       </a>
       ). During the 1970s, filmmakers increasingly depicted explicit sexual content and showed gunfight and battle scenes that included graphic images of bloody deaths –  a good example of this is
       <a href="/wiki/Wes_Craven" title="Wes Craven">
        Wes Craven
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Last_House_on_the_Left_(1972_film)" title="The Last House on the Left (1972 film)">
         The Last House on the Left
        </a>
       </i>
       (1972).
      </p>
      <p>
       Post-classical cinema is the changing methods of storytelling of the New Hollywood producers. The new methods of
       <a href="/wiki/Drama" title="Drama">
        drama
       </a>
       and characterization played upon audience expectations acquired during the classical/Golden Age period: story chronology may be scrambled, storylines may feature unsettling "
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twist_ending" title="Twist ending">
        twist endings
       </a>
       ", main characters may behave in a morally ambiguous fashion, and the lines between the
       <a href="/wiki/Antagonist" title="Antagonist">
        antagonist
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Protagonist" title="Protagonist">
        protagonist
       </a>
       may be blurred. The beginnings of post-classical storytelling may be seen in 1940s and 1950s
       <a href="/wiki/Film_noir" title="Film noir">
        film noir
       </a>
       films, in films such as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Rebel_Without_a_Cause" title="Rebel Without a Cause">
         Rebel Without a Cause
        </a>
       </i>
       (1955), and in Hitchcock's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Psycho_(1960_film)" title="Psycho (1960 film)">
         Psycho
        </a>
       </i>
       . 1971 marked the release of controversial films like
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Straw_Dogs_(1971_film)" title="Straw Dogs (1971 film)">
         Straw Dogs
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/A_Clockwork_Orange_(film)" title="A Clockwork Orange (film)">
         A Clockwork Orange
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_French_Connection_(film)" title="The French Connection (film)">
         The French Connection
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Dirty_Harry" title="Dirty Harry">
         Dirty Harry
        </a>
       </i>
       . This sparked heated controversy over the perceived escalation of violence in cinema.
      </p>
      <p>
       During the 1970s, a new group of American filmmakers emerged, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Martin_Scorsese" title="Martin Scorsese">
        Martin Scorsese
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Francis_Ford_Coppola" title="Francis Ford Coppola">
        Francis Ford Coppola
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/George_Lucas" title="George Lucas">
        George Lucas
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Woody_Allen" title="Woody Allen">
        Woody Allen
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Terrence_Malick" title="Terrence Malick">
        Terrence Malick
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Robert_Altman" title="Robert Altman">
        Robert Altman
       </a>
       . This coincided with the increasing popularity of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auteur_theory" title="Auteur theory">
        auteur theory
       </a>
       in film literature and the media, which posited that a film director's films express their personal vision and creative insights. The development of the auteur style of filmmaking helped to give these directors far greater control over their projects than would have been possible in earlier eras. This led to some great critical and commercial successes, like Scorsese's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Taxi_Driver" title="Taxi Driver">
         Taxi Driver
        </a>
       </i>
       , Coppola's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Godfather" title="The Godfather">
         The Godfather
        </a>
       </i>
       films,
       <a href="/wiki/William_Friedkin" title="William Friedkin">
        William Friedkin
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Exorcist_(film)" title="The Exorcist (film)">
         The Exorcist
        </a>
       </i>
       , Altman's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Nashville_(film)" title="Nashville (film)">
         Nashville
        </a>
       </i>
       , Allen's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Annie_Hall" title="Annie Hall">
         Annie Hall
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_(film)" title="Manhattan (film)">
         Manhattan
        </a>
       </i>
       , Malick's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Badlands_(film)" title="Badlands (film)">
         Badlands
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Days_of_Heaven" title="Days of Heaven">
         Days of Heaven
        </a>
       </i>
       , and Polish immigrant
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Polanski" title="Roman Polanski">
        Roman Polanski
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Chinatown_(1974_film)" title="Chinatown (1974 film)">
         Chinatown
        </a>
       </i>
       . It also, however, resulted in some failures, including
       <a href="/wiki/Peter_Bogdanovich" title="Peter Bogdanovich">
        Peter Bogdanovich
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/At_Long_Last_Love" title="At Long Last Love">
         At Long Last Love
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Michael_Cimino" title="Michael Cimino">
        Michael Cimino
       </a>
       's hugely expensive Western epic
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Heaven%27s_Gate_(film)" title="Heaven's Gate (film)">
         Heaven's Gate
        </a>
       </i>
       , which helped to bring about the demise of its backer,
       <a href="/wiki/United_Artists" title="United Artists">
        United Artists
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The financial disaster of
       <i>
        Heaven's Gate
       </i>
       marked the end of the visionary "auteur" directors of the "New Hollywood", who had unrestrained creative and financial freedom to develop films. The phenomenal success in the 1970s of
       <a href="/wiki/Steven_Spielberg" title="Steven Spielberg">
        Spielberg
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Jaws_(film)" title="Jaws (film)">
         Jaws
        </a>
       </i>
       originated the concept of the modern "
       <a href="/wiki/Blockbuster_(entertainment)" title="Blockbuster (entertainment)">
        blockbuster
       </a>
       ". However, the enormous success of George Lucas' 1977 film
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Star_Wars_(film)" title="Star Wars (film)">
         Star Wars
        </a>
       </i>
       led to much more than just the popularization of blockbuster film-making. The film's revolutionary use of special effects, sound editing and music had led it to become widely regarded as one of the single most important films in the medium's history, as well as the most influential film of the 1970s. Hollywood studios increasingly focused on producing a smaller number of very large budget films with massive marketing and promotional campaigns. This trend had already been foreshadowed by the commercial success of
       <a href="/wiki/Disaster_film" title="Disaster film">
        disaster films
       </a>
       such as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Poseidon_Adventure_(1972_film)" title="The Poseidon Adventure (1972 film)">
         The Poseidon Adventure
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Towering_Inferno_(film)" title="The Towering Inferno (film)">
         The Towering Inferno
        </a>
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       During the mid-1970s, more pornographic theatres, euphemistically called "adult cinemas", were established, and the legal production of
       <a href="/wiki/Hardcore_pornography" title="Hardcore pornography">
        hardcore pornographic
       </a>
       films began. Porn films such as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Deep_Throat_(film)" title="Deep Throat (film)">
         Deep Throat
        </a>
       </i>
       and its star
       <a href="/wiki/Linda_Lovelace" title="Linda Lovelace">
        Linda Lovelace
       </a>
       became something of a popular culture phenomenon and resulted in a spate of similar sex films. The porn cinemas finally died out during the 1980s, when the popularization of the home
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/VCR" title="VCR">
        VCR
       </a>
       and pornography videotapes allowed audiences to watch sex films at home. In the early 1970s, English-language audiences became more aware of the new
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_German" title="West German">
        West German
       </a>
       cinema, with
       <a href="/wiki/Werner_Herzog" title="Werner Herzog">
        Werner Herzog
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Rainer_Werner_Fassbinder" title="Rainer Werner Fassbinder">
        Rainer Werner Fassbinder
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Wim_Wenders" title="Wim Wenders">
        Wim Wenders
       </a>
       among its leading exponents.
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/World_cinema" title="World cinema">
        world cinema
       </a>
       , the 1970s saw a dramatic increase in the popularity of
       <a href="/wiki/Martial_arts_film" title="Martial arts film">
        martial arts films
       </a>
       , largely due to its reinvention by
       <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Lee" title="Bruce Lee">
        Bruce Lee
       </a>
       , who departed from the artistic style of traditional
       <a href="/wiki/Chinese_martial_arts" title="Chinese martial arts">
        Chinese martial arts
       </a>
       films and added a much greater sense of realism to them with his
       <a href="/wiki/Jeet_Kune_Do" title="Jeet Kune Do">
        Jeet Kune Do
       </a>
       style. This began with
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Big_Boss" title="The Big Boss">
         The Big Boss
        </a>
       </i>
       (1971), which was a major success across
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asian_cinema" title="Asian cinema">
        Asia
       </a>
       . However, he didn't gain fame in the
       <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">
        Western world
       </a>
       until shortly after his death in 1973, when
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Enter_the_Dragon" title="Enter the Dragon">
         Enter the Dragon
        </a>
       </i>
       was released. The film went on to become the most successful martial arts film in cinematic history, popularized the martial arts film genre across the world, and cemented Bruce Lee's status as a cultural icon.
       <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_action_cinema" title="Hong Kong action cinema">
        Hong Kong action cinema
       </a>
       , however, was in decline due to a wave of "
       <a href="/wiki/Bruceploitation" title="Bruceploitation">
        Bruceploitation
       </a>
       " films. This trend eventually came to an end in 1978 with the martial arts
       <a href="/wiki/Comedy_film" title="Comedy film">
        comedy films
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Snake_in_the_Eagle%27s_Shadow" title="Snake in the Eagle's Shadow">
         Snake in the Eagle's Shadow
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Drunken_Master" title="Drunken Master">
         Drunken Master
        </a>
       </i>
       , directed by
       <a href="/wiki/Yuen_Woo-ping" title="Yuen Woo-ping">
        Yuen Woo-ping
       </a>
       and starring
       <a href="/wiki/Jackie_Chan" title="Jackie Chan">
        Jackie Chan
       </a>
       , laying the foundations for the rise of Hong Kong action cinema in the 1980s.
      </p>
      <p>
       While the
       <a href="/wiki/Musical_film" title="Musical film">
        musical film
       </a>
       genre had declined in Hollywood by this time, musical films were quickly gaining popularity in the
       <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_India" title="Cinema of India">
        cinema of India
       </a>
       , where the term "
       <a href="/wiki/Bollywood" title="Bollywood">
        Bollywood
       </a>
       " was coined for the growing
       <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">
        Hindi
       </a>
       film industry in
       <a href="/wiki/Mumbai" title="Mumbai">
        Bombay
       </a>
       (now Mumbai) that ended up dominating
       <a href="/wiki/South_Asian_cinema" title="South Asian cinema">
        South Asian cinema
       </a>
       , overtaking the more critically acclaimed
       <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_West_Bengal" title="Cinema of West Bengal">
        Bengali film industry
       </a>
       in popularity. Hindi filmmakers combined the Hollywood musical formula with the conventions of ancient
       <a href="/wiki/Theatre_of_India" title="Theatre of India">
        Indian theatre
       </a>
       to create a new film genre called "
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Masala_(film_genre)" title="Masala (film genre)">
        Masala
       </a>
       ", which dominated Indian cinema throughout the late 20th century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These "Masala" films portrayed
       <a href="/wiki/Action_film" title="Action film">
        action
       </a>
       , comedy,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drama_film" title="Drama film">
        drama
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_film" title="Romance film">
        romance
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Melodrama" title="Melodrama">
        melodrama
       </a>
       all at once, with "
       <a href="/wiki/Filmi" title="Filmi">
        filmi
       </a>
       " song and dance routines thrown in. This trend began with films directed by
       <a href="/wiki/Manmohan_Desai" title="Manmohan Desai">
        Manmohan Desai
       </a>
       and starring
       <a href="/wiki/Amitabh_Bachchan" title="Amitabh Bachchan">
        Amitabh Bachchan
       </a>
       , who remains one of the most popular
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Film_star" title="Film star">
        film stars
       </a>
       in South Asia. The most popular Indian film of all time was
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Sholay" title="Sholay">
         Sholay
        </a>
       </i>
       (1975), a "Masala" film inspired by a real-life
       <a href="/wiki/Dacoity" title="Dacoity">
        dacoit
       </a>
       as well as Kurosawa's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Seven_Samurai" title="Seven Samurai">
         Seven Samurai
        </a>
       </i>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Spaghetti_Western" title="Spaghetti Western">
        Spaghetti Westerns
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The end of the decade saw the first major international marketing of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Australian" title="Australian">
        Australian
       </a>
       cinema, as
       <a href="/wiki/Peter_Weir" title="Peter Weir">
        Peter Weir
       </a>
       's films
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Picnic_at_Hanging_Rock_(film)" title="Picnic at Hanging Rock (film)">
         Picnic at Hanging Rock
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Last_Wave" title="The Last Wave">
         The Last Wave
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Fred_Schepisi" title="Fred Schepisi">
        Fred Schepisi
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Chant_of_Jimmie_Blacksmith" title="The Chant of Jimmie Blacksmith">
         The Chant of Jimmie Blacksmith
        </a>
       </i>
       gained critical acclaim. In 1979, Australian filmmaker
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Miller_(producer)" title="George Miller (producer)">
        George Miller
       </a>
       also garnered international attention for his violent, low-budget action film
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Mad_Max" title="Mad Max">
         Mad Max
        </a>
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="1980s">
        1980s
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: 1980s">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/1980s_in_film" title="1980s in film">
        1980s in film
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Expendables_2_Cast_Roster.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1794" data-file-width="1306" height="343" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/The_Expendables_2_Cast_Roster.jpg/250px-The_Expendables_2_Cast_Roster.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/The_Expendables_2_Cast_Roster.jpg/375px-The_Expendables_2_Cast_Roster.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/The_Expendables_2_Cast_Roster.jpg/500px-The_Expendables_2_Cast_Roster.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Expendables_2_Cast_Roster.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Ensemble cast of action stars of the 80s, including (left to right)
         <a href="/wiki/Sylvester_Stallone" title="Sylvester Stallone">
          Sylvester Stallone
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Jason_Statham" title="Jason Statham">
          Jason Statham
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Jet_Li" title="Jet Li">
          Jet Li
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Dolph_Lundgren" title="Dolph Lundgren">
          Dolph Lundgren
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Norris" title="Chuck Norris">
          Chuck Norris
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Jean-Claude_Van_Damme" title="Jean-Claude Van Damme">
          Jean-Claude Van Damme
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Willis" title="Bruce Willis">
          Bruce Willis
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Schwarzenegger" title="Arnold Schwarzenegger">
          Arnold Schwarzenegger
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Terry_Crews" title="Terry Crews">
          Terry Crews
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       During the 1980s, audiences began increasingly watching films on their home
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/VCR" title="VCR">
        VCRs
       </a>
       . In the early part of that decade, the
       <a href="/wiki/Film_studio" title="Film studio">
        film studios
       </a>
       tried legal action to ban home ownership of VCRs as a violation of
       <a href="/wiki/Copyright" title="Copyright">
        copyright
       </a>
       , which proved unsuccessful. Eventually, the sale and rental of films on
       <a href="/wiki/Home_video" title="Home video">
        home video
       </a>
       became a significant "second venue" for exhibition of films, and an additional source of revenue for the film industries.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/George_Lucas" title="George Lucas">
        Lucas
       </a>
       –
       <a href="/wiki/Steven_Spielberg" title="Steven Spielberg">
        Spielberg
       </a>
       combine would dominate "Hollywood" cinema for much of the 1980s, and lead to much imitation. Two follow-ups to
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Star_Wars_(film)" title="Star Wars (film)">
         Star Wars
        </a>
       </i>
       , three to
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Jaws_(film)" title="Jaws (film)">
         Jaws
        </a>
       </i>
       , and three
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Indiana_Jones" title="Indiana Jones">
         Indiana Jones
        </a>
       </i>
       films helped to make sequels of successful films more of an expectation than ever before. Lucas also launched
       <a href="/wiki/THX" title="THX">
        THX Ltd
       </a>
       , a division of
       <a href="/wiki/Lucasfilm" title="Lucasfilm">
        Lucasfilm
       </a>
       in 1982,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       while Spielberg enjoyed one of the decade's greatest successes in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/E.T._the_Extra-Terrestrial" title="E.T. the Extra-Terrestrial">
         E.T. the Extra-Terrestrial
        </a>
       </i>
       the same year. 1982 also saw the release of Disney's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Tron" title="Tron">
         Tron
        </a>
       </i>
       which was one of the first films from a major studio to use
       <a href="/wiki/Computer_graphics" title="Computer graphics">
        computer graphics
       </a>
       extensively. American independent cinema struggled more during the decade, although
       <a href="/wiki/Martin_Scorsese" title="Martin Scorsese">
        Martin Scorsese
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Raging_Bull" title="Raging Bull">
         Raging Bull
        </a>
       </i>
       (1980),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/After_Hours_(film)" title="After Hours (film)">
         After Hours
        </a>
       </i>
       (1985), and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_King_of_Comedy_(film)" title="The King of Comedy (film)">
         The King of Comedy
        </a>
       </i>
       (1983) helped to establish him as one of the most critically acclaimed American film makers of the era. Also during 1983
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Scarface_(1983_film)" title="Scarface (1983 film)">
         Scarface
        </a>
       </i>
       was released, which was very profitable and resulted in even greater fame for its leading actor
       <a href="/wiki/Al_Pacino" title="Al Pacino">
        Al Pacino
       </a>
       . Probably the most successful film commercially was vended during 1989:
       <a href="/wiki/Tim_Burton" title="Tim Burton">
        Tim Burton
       </a>
       's version of
       <a href="/wiki/Bob_Kane" title="Bob Kane">
        Bob Kane
       </a>
       's creation,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Batman_(1989_film)" title="Batman (1989 film)">
         Batman
        </a>
       </i>
       , exceeded box-office records.
       <a href="/wiki/Jack_Nicholson" title="Jack Nicholson">
        Jack Nicholson
       </a>
       's portrayal of the demented
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joker_(comics)" title="Joker (comics)">
        Joker
       </a>
       earned him a total of $60,000,000 after figuring in his percentage of the gross.
      </p>
      <p>
       British cinema was given a boost during the early 1980s by the arrival of
       <a href="/wiki/David_Puttnam" title="David Puttnam">
        David Puttnam
       </a>
       's company
       <a href="/wiki/Goldcrest_Films" title="Goldcrest Films">
        Goldcrest Films
       </a>
       . The films
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Chariots_of_Fire" title="Chariots of Fire">
         Chariots of Fire
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Gandhi_(film)" title="Gandhi (film)">
         Gandhi
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Killing_Fields_(film)" title="The Killing Fields (film)">
         The Killing Fields
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_Room_with_a_View_(1986_film)" title="A Room with a View (1986 film)">
         A Room with a View
        </a>
       </i>
       appealed to a "middlebrow" audience which was increasingly being ignored by the major Hollywood studios. While the films of the 1970s had helped to define modern
       <a href="/wiki/Blockbuster_(entertainment)" title="Blockbuster (entertainment)">
        blockbuster
       </a>
       motion pictures, the way "Hollywood" released its films would now change. Films, for the most part, would premiere in a wider number of theatres, although, to this day, some films still premiere using the route of the
       <a href="/wiki/Roadshow_theatrical_release" title="Roadshow theatrical release">
        limited/roadshow release system
       </a>
       . Against some expectations, the rise of the multiplex cinema did not allow less mainstream films to be shown, but simply allowed the major blockbusters to be given an even greater number of screenings. However, films that had been overlooked in cinemas were increasingly being given a second chance on home video.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cannes_2014_2.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="439" data-file-width="800" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Cannes_2014_2.jpg/300px-Cannes_2014_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Cannes_2014_2.jpg/450px-Cannes_2014_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Cannes_2014_2.jpg/600px-Cannes_2014_2.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cannes_2014_2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Ensemble cast of action stars end of the 80's
         <a href="/wiki/Sylvester_Stallone" title="Sylvester Stallone">
          Sylvester Stallone
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Jason_Statham" title="Jason Statham">
          Jason Statham
