         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Banderas" title="Antonio Banderas">
          Antonio Banderas
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Jet_Li" title="Jet Li">
          Jet Li
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Wesley_Snipes" title="Wesley Snipes">
          Wesley Snipes
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Dolph_Lundgren" title="Dolph Lundgren">
          Dolph Lundgren
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Kelsey_Grammer" title="Kelsey Grammer">
          Kelsey Grammer
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Randy_Couture" title="Randy Couture">
          Randy Couture
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Terry_Crews" title="Terry Crews">
          Terry Crews
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Kellan_Lutz" title="Kellan Lutz">
          Kellan Lutz
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Ronda_Rousey" title="Ronda Rousey">
          Ronda Rousey
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Glen_Powell" title="Glen Powell">
          Glen Powell
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Victor_Ortiz" title="Victor Ortiz">
          Victor Ortiz
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Mel_Gibson" title="Mel Gibson">
          Mel Gibson
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Harrison_Ford" title="Harrison Ford">
          Harrison Ford
         </a>
         , and
         <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Schwarzenegger" title="Arnold Schwarzenegger">
          Arnold Schwarzenegger
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       During the 1980s,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_cinema" title="Japanese cinema">
        Japanese cinema
       </a>
       experienced a revival, largely due to the success of
       <a href="/wiki/Anime" title="Anime">
        anime
       </a>
       films. At the beginning of the 1980s,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Space_Battleship_Yamato" title="Space Battleship Yamato">
         Space Battleship Yamato
        </a>
       </i>
       (1973) and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Mobile_Suit_Gundam" title="Mobile Suit Gundam">
         Mobile Suit Gundam
        </a>
       </i>
       (1979), both of which were unsuccessful as television series, were remade as films and became hugely successful in Japan. In particular,
       <i>
        Mobile Suit Gundam
       </i>
       sparked the
       <a href="/wiki/Gundam" title="Gundam">
        Gundam
       </a>
       franchise of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Real_Robot" title="Real Robot">
        Real Robot
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Mecha" title="Mecha">
        mecha
       </a>
       anime. The success of
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Super_Dimension_Fortress_Macross:_Do_You_Remember_Love%3F" title="The Super Dimension Fortress Macross: Do You Remember Love?">
         Macross: Do You Remember Love?
        </a>
       </i>
       also sparked a
       <a href="/wiki/Macross" title="Macross">
        Macross
       </a>
       franchise of mecha anime. This was also the decade when
       <a href="/wiki/Studio_Ghibli" title="Studio Ghibli">
        Studio Ghibli
       </a>
       was founded. The studio produced
       <a href="/wiki/Hayao_Miyazaki" title="Hayao Miyazaki">
        Hayao Miyazaki
       </a>
       's first
       <a href="/wiki/Fantasy_film" title="Fantasy film">
        fantasy films
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Nausica%C3%A4_of_the_Valley_of_the_Wind_(film)" title="Nausicaä of the Valley of the Wind (film)">
         Nausicaä of the Valley of the Wind
        </a>
       </i>
       (1984) and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Castle_in_the_Sky" title="Castle in the Sky">
         Castle in the Sky
        </a>
       </i>
       (1986), as well as
       <a href="/wiki/Isao_Takahata" title="Isao Takahata">
        Isao Takahata
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Grave_of_the_Fireflies" title="Grave of the Fireflies">
         Grave of the Fireflies
        </a>
       </i>
       (1988), all of which were very successful in Japan and received worldwide critical acclaim.
       <a href="/wiki/Original_video_animation" title="Original video animation">
        Original video animation
       </a>
       (OVA) films also began during this decade; the most influential of these early OVA films was
       <a href="/wiki/Noboru_Ishiguro" title="Noboru Ishiguro">
        Noboru Ishiguro
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/Cyberpunk" title="Cyberpunk">
        cyberpunk
       </a>
       film
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Megazone_23" title="Megazone 23">
         Megazone 23
        </a>
       </i>
       (1985). The most famous anime film of this decade was
       <a href="/wiki/Katsuhiro_Otomo" title="Katsuhiro Otomo">
        Katsuhiro Otomo
       </a>
       's cyberpunk film
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Akira_(1988_film)" title="Akira (1988 film)">
         Akira
        </a>
       </i>
       (1988), which although initially unsuccessful at Japanese theaters, went on to become an international success.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_action_cinema" title="Hong Kong action cinema">
        Hong Kong action cinema
       </a>
       , which was in a state of decline due to endless
       <a href="/wiki/Bruceploitation" title="Bruceploitation">
        Bruceploitation
       </a>
       films after the death of
       <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Lee" title="Bruce Lee">
        Bruce Lee
       </a>
       , also experienced a revival in the 1980s, largely due to the reinvention of the
       <a href="/wiki/Action_film" title="Action film">
        action film
       </a>
       genre by
       <a href="/wiki/Jackie_Chan" title="Jackie Chan">
        Jackie Chan
       </a>
       . He had previously combined the
       <a href="/wiki/Comedy_film" title="Comedy film">
        comedy film
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Martial_arts_film" title="Martial arts film">
        martial arts film
       </a>
       genres successfully in the 1978 films
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Snake_in_the_Eagle%27s_Shadow" title="Snake in the Eagle's Shadow">
         Snake in the Eagle's Shadow
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Drunken_Master" title="Drunken Master">
         Drunken Master
        </a>
       </i>
       . The next step he took was in combining this comedy martial arts genre with a new emphasis on elaborate and highly dangerous
       <a href="/wiki/Stunt" title="Stunt">
        stunts
       </a>
       , reminiscent of the
       <a href="/wiki/Silent_film" title="Silent film">
        silent film
       </a>
       era. The first film in this new style of action cinema was
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Project_A" title="Project A">
         Project A
        </a>
       </i>
       (1983), which saw the formation of the
       <a href="/wiki/Jackie_Chan_Stunt_Team" title="Jackie Chan Stunt Team">
        Jackie Chan Stunt Team
       </a>
       as well as the "Three Brothers" (Chan,
       <a href="/wiki/Sammo_Hung" title="Sammo Hung">
        Sammo Hung
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Yuen_Biao" title="Yuen Biao">
        Yuen Biao
       </a>
       ). The film added elaborate, dangerous stunts to the fights and
       <a href="/wiki/Slapstick" title="Slapstick">
        slapstick
       </a>
       humor, and became a huge success throughout the
       <a href="/wiki/Far_East" title="Far East">
        Far East
       </a>
       . As a result, Chan continued this trend with martial arts action films containing even more elaborate and dangerous stunts, including
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wheels_on_Meals" title="Wheels on Meals">
         Wheels on Meals
        </a>
       </i>
       (1984),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Police_Story_(1985_film)" title="Police Story (1985 film)">
         Police Story
        </a>
       </i>
       (1985),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Armour_of_God_(film)" title="Armour of God (film)">
         Armour of God
        </a>
       </i>
       (1986),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Project_A_Part_II" title="Project A Part II">
         Project A Part II
        </a>
       </i>
       (1987),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Police_Story_2" title="Police Story 2">
         Police Story 2
        </a>
       </i>
       (1988), and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Dragons_Forever" title="Dragons Forever">
         Dragons Forever
        </a>
       </i>
       (1988). Other new trends which began in the 1980s were the "
       <a href="/wiki/Girls_with_guns" title="Girls with guns">
        girls with guns
       </a>
       " subgenre, for which
       <a href="/wiki/Michelle_Yeoh" title="Michelle Yeoh">
        Michelle Yeoh
       </a>
       gained fame; and especially the "
       <a href="/wiki/Heroic_bloodshed" title="Heroic bloodshed">
        heroic bloodshed
       </a>
       " genre, revolving around
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triad_(underground_societies)" title="Triad (underground societies)">
        Triads
       </a>
       , largely pioneered by
       <a href="/wiki/John_Woo" title="John Woo">
        John Woo
       </a>
       and for which
       <a href="/wiki/Chow_Yun-fat" title="Chow Yun-fat">
        Chow Yun-fat
       </a>
       became famous. These Hong Kong action trends were later adopted by many Hollywood action films in the 1990s and 2000s.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="1990s">
        1990s
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: 1990s">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/1990s_in_film" title="1990s in film">
        1990s in film
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The early 1990s saw the development of a commercially successful independent cinema in the United States. Although cinema was increasingly dominated by special-effects films such as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Terminator_2:_Judgment_Day" title="Terminator 2: Judgment Day">
         Terminator 2: Judgment Day
        </a>
       </i>
       (1991),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Jurassic_Park_(film)" title="Jurassic Park (film)">
         Jurassic Park
        </a>
       </i>
       (1993) and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Titanic_(1997_film)" title="Titanic (1997 film)">
         Titanic
        </a>
       </i>
       (1997), the latter of which became the highest-grossing film of all time at the time up until
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Avatar_(2009_film)" title="Avatar (2009 film)">
         Avatar
        </a>
       </i>
       (2009), also directed by James Cameron, independent films like
       <a href="/wiki/Steven_Soderbergh" title="Steven Soderbergh">
        Steven Soderbergh
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Sex,_Lies,_and_Videotape" title="Sex, Lies, and Videotape">
         Sex, Lies, and Videotape
        </a>
       </i>
       (1989) and
       <a href="/wiki/Quentin_Tarantino" title="Quentin Tarantino">
        Quentin Tarantino
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Reservoir_Dogs" title="Reservoir Dogs">
         Reservoir Dogs
        </a>
       </i>
       (1992) had significant commercial success both at the cinema and on home video. Filmmakers associated with the Danish film movement
       <a href="/wiki/Dogme_95" title="Dogme 95">
        Dogme 95
       </a>
       introduced a manifesto aimed to purify filmmaking. Its first few films gained worldwide critical acclaim, after which the movement slowly faded out.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1995cinema_admissions.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="625" data-file-width="1425" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/1995cinema_admissions.png/250px-1995cinema_admissions.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/1995cinema_admissions.png/375px-1995cinema_admissions.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/1995cinema_admissions.png/500px-1995cinema_admissions.png 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1995cinema_admissions.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Cinema admissions in 1995
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Major American studios began to create their own
       <a href="/wiki/Independent_film" title="Independent film">
        "independent" production companies
       </a>
       to finance and produce non-mainstream fare. One of the most successful independents of the 1990s,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miramax_Films" title="Miramax Films">
        Miramax Films
       </a>
       , was bought by Disney the year before the release of Tarantino's runaway hit
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulp_Fiction_(film)" title="Pulp Fiction (film)">
         Pulp Fiction
        </a>
       </i>
       in 1994. The same year marked the beginning of film and video distribution online. Animated films aimed at family audiences also regained their popularity, with Disney's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Beauty_and_the_Beast_(1991_film)" title="Beauty and the Beast (1991 film)">
         Beauty and the Beast
        </a>
       </i>
       (1991),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Aladdin_(1992_Disney_film)" title="Aladdin (1992 Disney film)">
         Aladdin
        </a>
       </i>
       (1992), and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Lion_King" title="The Lion King">
         The Lion King
        </a>
       </i>
       (1994). During 1995, the first feature length
       <a href="/wiki/Computer_animation" title="Computer animation">
        computer-animated
       </a>
       feature,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Toy_Story" title="Toy Story">
         Toy Story
        </a>
       </i>
       , was produced by
       <a href="/wiki/Pixar" title="Pixar">
        Pixar Animation Studios
       </a>
       and released by Disney. After the success of Toy Story, computer animation would grow to become the dominant technique for feature length animation, which would allow competing film companies such as
       <a href="/wiki/DreamWorks_Animation" title="DreamWorks Animation">
        DreamWorks Animation
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/20th_Century_Fox" title="20th Century Fox">
        20th Century Fox
       </a>
       to effectively compete with Disney with successful films of their own. During the late 1990s, another cinematic transition began, from physical film stock to
       <a href="/wiki/Digital_cinema" title="Digital cinema">
        digital cinema
       </a>
       technology. Meanwhile,
       <a href="/wiki/DVD" title="DVD">
        DVDs
       </a>
       became the new standard for consumer video, replacing VHS tapes.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_years">
        Recent years
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_film&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Recent years">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/2000s_in_film" title="2000s in film">
        2000s in film
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/2010s_in_film" title="2010s in film">
        2010s in film
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Documentary_film" title="Documentary film">
        documentary film
       </a>
       also rose as a commercial genre for perhaps the first time, with the success of films such as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/March_of_the_Penguins" title="March of the Penguins">
         March of the Penguins
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Michael_Moore" title="Michael Moore">
        Michael Moore
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Bowling_for_Columbine" title="Bowling for Columbine">
         Bowling for Columbine
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Fahrenheit_9/11" title="Fahrenheit 9/11">
         Fahrenheit 9/11
        </a>
       </i>
       . A new genre was created with
       <a href="/wiki/Martin_Kunert" title="Martin Kunert">
        Martin Kunert
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Eric_Manes" title="Eric Manes">
        Eric Manes
       </a>
       '
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Voices_of_Iraq" title="Voices of Iraq">
         Voices of Iraq
        </a>
       </i>
       , when 150 inexpensive DV cameras were distributed across Iraq, transforming ordinary people into collaborative filmmakers. The success of
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Gladiator_(2000_film)" title="Gladiator (2000 film)">
         Gladiator
        </a>
       </i>
       led to a revival of interest in
       <a href="/wiki/Epic_film" title="Epic film">
        epic cinema
       </a>
       , and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Moulin_Rouge!" title="Moulin Rouge!">
         Moulin Rouge!
        </a>
       </i>
       renewed interest in
       <a href="/wiki/Musical_film" title="Musical film">
        musical cinema
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Home_cinema" title="Home cinema">
        Home theatre
       </a>
       systems became increasingly sophisticated, as did some of the special edition
       <a href="/wiki/DVD" title="DVD">
        DVDs
       </a>
       designed to be shown on them.
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Lord_of_the_Rings_(film_series)" title="The Lord of the Rings (film series)">
         The Lord of the Rings trilogy
        </a>
       </i>
       was released on DVD in both the theatrical version and in a special extended version intended only for home cinema audiences.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2001, the
       <a href="/wiki/Harry_Potter_(film_series)" title="Harry Potter (film series)">
        <i>
         Harry Potter
        </i>
        film series
       </a>
       began, and by its end in 2011, it had become the highest-grossing film franchise of all time until the Marvel Cinematic Universe passed it in 2015.
      </p>
      <p>
       More films were also being released simultaneously to
       <a href="/wiki/IMAX" title="IMAX">
        IMAX
       </a>
       cinema, the first was in 2002's Disney animation
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Treasure_Planet" title="Treasure Planet">
         Treasure Planet
        </a>
       </i>
       ; and the first live action was in 2003's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Matrix_Revolutions" title="The Matrix Revolutions">
         The Matrix Revolutions
        </a>
       </i>
       and a re-release of
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Matrix_Reloaded" title="The Matrix Reloaded">
         The Matrix Reloaded
        </a>
       </i>
       . Later in the decade,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Dark_Knight_(film)" title="The Dark Knight (film)">
         The Dark Knight
        </a>
       </i>
       was the first major feature film to have been at least partially shot in IMAX technology.
      </p>
      <p>
       There has been an increasing globalization of cinema during this decade, with foreign-language films gaining popularity in English-speaking markets. Examples of such films include
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Crouching_Tiger,_Hidden_Dragon" title="Crouching Tiger, Hidden Dragon">
         Crouching Tiger, Hidden Dragon
        </a>
       </i>
       (Mandarin),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Am%C3%A9lie" title="Amélie">
         Amélie
        </a>
       </i>
       (French),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Lagaan" title="Lagaan">
         Lagaan
        </a>
       </i>
       (Hindi),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Spirited_Away" title="Spirited Away">
         Spirited Away
        </a>
       </i>
       (Japanese),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/City_of_God_(2002_film)" title="City of God (2002 film)">
         City of God
        </a>
       </i>
       (Brazilian Portuguese),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Passion_of_the_Christ" title="The Passion of the Christ">
         The Passion of the Christ
        </a>
       </i>
       (Aramaic),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Apocalypto" title="Apocalypto">
         Apocalypto
        </a>
       </i>
       (Mayan) and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Inglourious_Basterds" title="Inglourious Basterds">
         Inglourious Basterds
        </a>
       </i>
       (multiple European languages). Italy is the most awarded country at the
       <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Foreign_Language_Film" title="Academy Award for Best Foreign Language Film">
        Academy Award for Best Foreign Language Film
       </a>
       , with 14 awards won, 3 Special Awards and 31
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Italian_submissions_for_the_Academy_Award_for_Best_Foreign_Language_Film" title="List of Italian submissions for the Academy Award for Best Foreign Language Film">
        nominations
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Recently there has been a revival in 3D film popularity the first being James Cameron's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Ghosts_of_the_Abyss" title="Ghosts of the Abyss">
         Ghosts of the Abyss
        </a>
       </i>
       which was released as the first full-length 3-D IMAX feature filmed with the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reality_Camera_System" title="Reality Camera System">
        Reality Camera System
       </a>
       . This camera system used the latest HD video cameras, not film, and was built for Cameron by Emmy nominated Director of Photography Vince Pace, to his specifications. The same camera system was used to film
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spy_Kids_3D:_Game_Over" title="Spy Kids 3D: Game Over">
         Spy Kids 3D: Game Over
        </a>
       </i>
       (2003),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Aliens_of_the_Deep" title="Aliens of the Deep">
         Aliens of the Deep
        </a>
       </i>
       IMAX (2005), and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Sharkboy_and_Lavagirl_in_3-D" title="The Adventures of Sharkboy and Lavagirl in 3-D">
         The Adventures of Sharkboy and Lavagirl in 3-D
        </a>
       </i>
       (2005).
      </p>
      <p>
       After James Cameron's 3D film
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Avatar_(2009_film)" title="Avatar (2009 film)">
         Avatar
        </a>
       </i>
       became the highest-grossing film of all time, 3D films have gained increasing popularity with many other films being released in 3D, with the best critical and financial successes being in the field of feature film animation such as
       <a href="/wiki/Universal_Pictures" title="Universal Pictures">
        Universal Pictures
       </a>
       /
       <a href="/wiki/Illumination_Entertainment" title="Illumination Entertainment">
        Illumination Entertainment
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Despicable_Me" title="Despicable Me">
         Despicable Me
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/DreamWorks_Animation" title="DreamWorks Animation">
        DreamWorks Animation
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/How_To_Train_Your_Dragon_(film)" title="How To Train Your Dragon (film)">
         How To Train Your Dragon
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Shrek_Forever_After" title="Shrek Forever After">
         Shrek Forever After
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Megamind" title="Megamind">
         Megamind
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <i>
        Avatar
       </i>
       is also note-worthy for pioneering highly sophisticated use of motion capture technology and influencing several other films such as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Rise_of_the_Planet_of_the_Apes" title="Rise of the Planet of the Apes">
         Rise of the Planet of the Apes
        </a>
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       As of 2010, the largest film industries by number of feature films produced are those of India, the United States, China, Nigeria and Japan.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Beginning in 2008 with
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Iron_Man_(2008_film)" title="Iron Man (2008 film)">
         Iron Man
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Dark_Knight_(film)" title="The Dark Knight (film)">
         The Dark Knight
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Superhero_film" title="Superhero film">
        superhero films
       </a>
       have greatly increased in popularity and financial success, with films based on
       <a href="/wiki/Marvel_Comics" title="Marvel Comics">
        Marvel
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/DC_Comics" title="DC Comics">
        DC comics
       </a>
       regularly being released every year up to the present.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
        <a href="#cite_note-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

            </i>
           </a>
           . Indiana University Press. pp. 143–153.
           <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">

           </a>

           </a>

        <a class="external text" href="http://www.silentera.com/PSFL/indexes/earlySoundFilms.html" rel="nofollow">
         List of Early Sound Films 1894–1929 at Silent Era website
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="external text" href="http://www.widescreenmuseum.com/widescreen/early-jan1930.htm" rel="nofollow">
         Early History of Wide Films
        </a>
        – American Cinematographer, January, 1930
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="external text" href="http://hiyaa.yolasite.com/hollywood.php" rel="nofollow">
         Hollywood Movies History
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="external text" href="http://textmex.blogspot.com/" rel="nofollow">
         Latinos in the movies
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="external text" href="http://www.widescreenmuseum.com/oldcolor/technicolor1.htm" rel="nofollow">
         Technicolor History
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20120717074134/http://www.beanblossom.in.us/larryy/cgi.html" rel="nofollow">
         A Brief, Early History of Computer Graphics in Film
        </a>
       </li>
      </ul>
